Divine Decree

6 min
The Dilemma of Dual Capacity and Its Refutation
Al-Nasafi presents a dilemma on divine and human power and begins a refutation using an analogy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the dilemma about whether both God and humans have power over the same act, and al-Nasafi's refutation of the view that denies God's power using the analogy of a paralyzed person.

Core Idea
If God gives the power to act, He must have power over that act Himself.

Having established that human acts are known to God, al-Nasafi now turns to the question of power. He presents a dilemma: either both God and the servant have power over the act, or only the servant does. He then refutes the second possibility by arguing that the one who grants power must have power over what he grants; otherwise, a paralyzed person could empower someone to walk.

Guided Path
1
Read the dilemma
Identify the two horns of the dilemma: either God and the servant both have power and each can create the act independently, or God's power is not certain over the act because it belongs to the servant.
The first view is attributed to a group; the second to the majority.
2
Understand the refutation
Al-Nasafi rejects the second view because God is the one who gives the servant the power to act. It is impossible for God to empower the servant over something God Himself cannot do.
The refutation is incomplete in the text but the logic is clear.
3
Analyze the analogy
Al-Nasafi uses the analogy of a paralyzed person (muqʿad). If the second view were correct, a paralyzed person could empower someone to walk – but that is absurd because the paralyzed person does not have the power to walk.
The analogy is cut off but the intended lesson is clear.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والذي يؤيد ما ذكرنا أن كل فعل من أفعال العبد عرض وهو قبل حدوثه وخروجه من العدم إلى الوجود كان معلوم الباري جل وعلا بلا خوف، وهو عندهم جميعًا شيء، وعند البصريين منهم عوض، وعند الجبائي إن كان عند الوجد حركة فهو في حال العدم أيضا حركة، ثم لا شك أن في حال عدمه معلوم الله تعالى، والله تعالى يعلمه شيئا في حال عدمه، وعندهم وعند البصريين يعلمه عرضا وعند الجبائي منهم يعلمه حركة. ثم الحال لا يخلو إما إن كانت لله تعالى عليه قدرة كما للعبد عليه قدرة ويصح من كل واحد منهما الانفراد بإيجاده كما هو مذهب طائفة منهم، وإما إن كانت قدرة الله تعالى غير متيقنة لما كان مقدورًا للعبد لاستحالة كون مقدور لقادرين كما هو مذهب جمهورهم، ولا وجه إلى هذا ؛ لأن الله تعالى هو الذي يتولى إعطاء العبد قدرة الفعل وهو المقدر له، ومن المحال أن يقدر ذات ما غيره على ما لا قدرة للمقدر عليه، ولو جاز ذا لجاز أن يقدر المقعد غيره على عين المشي الذي المقعد
And what supports what we have mentioned is that every action of the servant's actions is an accident (ʿaraḍ), and before its occurrence and its exit from non-existence to existence, it was known to the Exalted and High Creator without fear [uncertainty?]. And it is, according to all of them, a thing (shayʾ). According to the Basrans among them, it is a substitute (ʿiwaḍ). According to al-Jubbāʾī, if at the time of existence it is motion, then in the state of non-existence it is also motion. Then there is no doubt that in the state of its non-existence, it is an object of God's knowledge, and God knows it as a thing in the state of its non-existence. According to them and to the Basrans, He knows it as an accident; according to al-Jubbāʾī among them, He knows it as motion. Then the situation is not free from one of two possibilities: either God has power over it just as the servant has power over it, and it is valid for each of them to single-handedly bring it into existence, as is the doctrine of a group among them; or God's power is not certain over what is the object of the servant's power, because of the impossibility of one object of power belonging to two powerful agents, as is the doctrine of the majority of them. But there is no justification for this latter view, because God is the one who undertakes to give the servant the power of the act, and He is the one who empowers him for it. And it is impossible that one entity empower another over what the empowering one has no power over. If this were allowed, then it would be permissible for a disabled person [المقعد] to empower another over the very walking that the disabled person...
Source pages p. 884

Commentary
This passage is a supporting argument for the author's position on divine power and human acts. Al-Nasafi states that every human act is an accident (ʿaraḍ) that exists after non-existence. Before its creation, it was known to God as a 'thing' (shayʾ) according to different schools: the Basrans (Muʿtazila) call it a substitute (ʿiwaḍ), and al-Jubbāʾī says if the act is motion when found, it is also motion in non-existence. All agree it is known to God. Then the author presents a dilemma: either both God and the servant have power over the same act, and either can create it individually (a view of some); or God's power over it is not certain because it is the servant's object of power, since one object cannot be under two agents (the majority view). Al-Nasafi rejects the second view because God is the one who gives the servant the power to act, and it is impossible for one to empower another over something he himself cannot do. If that were possible, a paralyzed person could empower someone to walk the very walking the paralyzed person does not have. The argument is incomplete in the provided text, but the intended refutation is clear: since God is the ultimate creator, human acts cannot be independent objects of power.

Key Terms
قدرة
المقعد
مقدور

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that if both God and humans had power over the same act, that would be impossible (shirk).
Correction: Al-Nasafi does not reject the possibility of dual power in principle; he rejects the denial of God's power over human acts. His refutation shows that God must have power because He gives the power.

Checkpoint
According to al-Nasafi, what is the main flaw in the majority view that God's power is not certain over a human act?
A) It contradicts the fact that acts are accidents
B) It implies that God cannot empower the servant over something He Himself cannot do
C) It makes the act eternal
D) It denies divine foreknowledge

Key Takeaway
God's power over human acts is necessary because He grants the power to act.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you perform an action, do you truly feel that God's power is behind every movement and thought—or do you sometimes act as if you are the independent creator of your deeds? What would change in your prayer if you sincerely believed that God empowers the very act of bowing?
?
The analogy of the paralyzed person exposes the absurdity of thinking God could give power over something He lacks. In what areas of your life do you secretly believe that God is unable to help you—that He has 'no power' over your struggles? Will you repent of that unspoken doubt today?
?
Al-Nasafi argues that the majority view leads to an impossible conclusion. Have you ever clung to a comfortable theological position simply because it seemed safer, without facing its deeper contradictions? Are you willing to let the truth of God's absolute power unsettle your assumptions about your own abilities?

Distinguishing Will from Wish in Maturidi Kalam
Answer to objection: will vs. wish distinction

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Maturidi distinction between true will (irada) and wish (tamanni), and why creating wills for two beings for opposite actions does not entail a contradiction.

Core Idea
A will without its action is not a will—it is merely a wish.

This passage responds to an objection that if God creates a will for each of two beings to perform opposite actions, it would lead to contradiction. The answer clarifies that such a scenario involves wishes rather than true wills, because a will is defined by being accompanied by its action. This lesson unpacks that distinction and its theological significance.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage and its English translation carefully. Notice the objection and the refutation structure.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the objection
Understand the hypothetical problem: if God creates a will for two beings for opposite actions, it seems to demand that both actions occur, which is impossible.
3
Follow the refutation
The reply argues that the objection stems from ignorance of the true nature of will. The key point: creating opposite wills is impossible, just as creating opposite actions is impossible.
4
Grasp the will-wish distinction
The passage defines will as always accompanied by its action. If no action occurs, the inclination is not will but wish (tamanni). Thus the objector's scenario involves two wishes, not two wills.
This distinction resolves the contradiction.
5
Reflect on the promise
The author promises a fuller explanation of will later. This passage lays groundwork for that discussion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مريدًا ما خلق لكل واحد منهما إرادته من الفعل كان مؤديا إلى ذلك وهو محال. قيل: هذا السؤال تمويه منكم إن عرفتم حقيقة الإرادة وإلا فهو صادر عن الجهل بحقيقتها، وهذا لأن تخليق الإرادة لذاتين لفعلين متضادين في وقت واحد في محل واحد محال كتخليق فعلين، فإن الإرادة بدون الفعل محال وكل ما يظن إرادة إذا لم يطابقه الفعل ولم يقترن به فهو تمن وليس بإرادة فلا يكون كل واحد منهما إرادة بل ما قارنه الفعل فهو إرادة وما لم يقارنه الفعل فهو تمن على ما نبين حقيقة هذا إذا انتهينا إلى مسألة الإرادة إن شاء الله تعالى.
Willing what He created for each one of them His will from the action leads to that [contradiction] and it is impossible. It was said: This question is a deception from you if you know the reality of will, otherwise it arises from ignorance of its reality. This is because creating will for two beings for two opposite actions at one time in one place is impossible, like creating two actions. For will without the action is impossible, and everything that is thought to be will if the action does not correspond to it and is not associated with it, it is a wish and not a will. Thus neither of them is a will, rather that which is accompanied by the action is a will, and that which is not accompanied by the action is a wish, as we will explain the reality of this when we reach the question of will, God willing.
Source pages p. 886

Commentary
This passage is an objection and answer. The objector claims that if God created a will for each of two beings for opposite actions, it would lead to contradiction (since both wills would require fulfillment). The answer explains that this confusion arises from ignorance of the true nature of will. Creating wills for two beings for opposite actions at the same time is impossible, just as creating two opposite actions is impossible. Because will without the action is impossible; if the action does not accompany the will, it is not will but wish (tamanni). Therefore, the objector's scenario is not a case of two true wills, but of two wishes, which is not problematic. The author promises to clarify further in the discussion of will.

Key Terms
إرادة
تمن
تخليق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If God creates a will in someone for an action, that will must be fulfilled by God; otherwise it is a contradiction.
Correction: The will created in the servant is the servant's inclination. If the action does not occur, it was a wish, not a real will. Therefore, no contradiction arises from unfulfilled wills.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the status of a supposed will that is not accompanied by its action?
A) It is an invalid will but still called will.
B) It is a wish (tamanni) and not will.
C) It is a will that is impossible to create.
D) It is a will that will be fulfilled later.

Key Takeaway
True will is always accompanied by its action; otherwise it is merely a wish, and no contradiction arises from unfulfilled wishes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you claim to 'will' to obey Allah more but your actions don't change, what does this passage say about the reality of your intention? Are you comfortable with your wishes remaining wishes?
?
Identify one area in your life where your actions do not match your stated will. Based on this lesson, what is that intention really? What would it take for you to transform that wish into a true will?
?
If Allah's will is always fulfilled in action, what does that imply about your own relationship with will? Are you content with a gap between your desires and deeds, or are you ready to demand from yourself that your will be accompanied by action starting today?

Why God Cannot Create Power Without Being Powerful
Objection and answer comparing wish and power.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the analogy between God creating wish and God creating power fails, and how divine power is an essential attribute that cannot be separated from the agent.

Core Idea
Power is an attribute inseparable from its agent; wish is not.

This passage presents a subtle theological objection. The objector argues that since God can create a wish for an action without being a wisher, He should be able to create power for an action without being powerful. The answer unpacks the difference between wish and power, showing that power is an essential attribute of the agent, while wish is not. Let's follow the reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
Read the question posed by the objector. They draw an analogy between wish (tamanni) and power (qudra).
2
Grasp the distinction between wish and will
The answer explains that wish is separate from will and only occurs when the action does not happen. God's will is always effective, so wish is not applicable to Him.
Notice how wish relates to actions that do not occur.
3
See why the analogy fails
Power is an attribute that necessarily belongs to the agent. If God created power for an action, He would have that power Himself, which is impossible to deny. Therefore, the analogy is invalid.
This highlights the difference between divine and created attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قالوا: لما جاز أن يخلق الله التمني لفعل لا يكون متمنيًا لذلك الفعل لماذا لا يجوز أن يخلق قدرة على فعل لا يكون هو قادرا على ذلك الفعل؟ قيل: هذا السؤال فاسد لما مر أن التمني يمتاز عن الإرادة بوجود مفارقة الفعل إياه وعدم المفارقة، ولا يجوز أن لا يوجد ما يريده الله تعالى فلا يتصور منه التمني، فإن كان ذلك الفعل مما يوجد فما وجد من العبد إرادة وتعلقت به أيضا إرادة الله تعالى وإن كان ذلك الفعل مما لا يوجد فما حصل من العبد تمن لا إرادة والله تعالى كان مريدًا عدم ذلك الفعل لا وجوده فبعدم ذلك الفعل الذي يريد عدمه لا يكون متمنيًا بل يكون مريدًا عدمه، ألا ترى أنه لا يكون متمنيًا للتمني الذي خلقه فلا يكون متمنيًا للفعل الذي خلق له التمني، فأما فيما نحن فيه فيستحيل أن يخلق قدرة لا قدرة له عليها، فيستحيل أن يخلق قدرة على فعل لا قدرة له عليه، وفي التمني بخلافه والله الموفق.
If they say: Since it is permissible for God to create wish (tamanni) for an action without being a wisher for that action, why is it not permissible for Him to create power (qudra) for an action without being powerful over that action? It was said: This question is corrupt because it has passed that wish is distinguished from will by the occurrence of the action's separation from it and non-separation. It is not permissible that what God wills does not exist, so wish is not conceivable from Him. If that action is such that it exists, then what exists from the servant is will, and the will of God also attaches to it. If that action is such that it does not exist, then what occurs from the servant is wish, not will, and God is willing the non-existence of that action, not its existence. So due to the non-existence of that action which He wills not to exist, He is not a wisher but is willing its non-existence. Do you not see that He is not a wisher for the wish which He created, so He is not a wisher for the action for which He created the wish? As for our subject, it is impossible for Him to create a power over which He has no power, so it is impossible for Him to create power for an action over which He has no power. In the case of wish, it is the opposite. And God is the provider of success.
Source pages p. 886

Commentary
This passage is an objection and answer. The objector uses an analogy: since God can create wish (tamanni) for an action in someone without being a wisher for that action, why can He not create power (qudra) for an action without being powerful over it? The answer says this analogy is invalid. Wish is distinguished from will by the non-occurrence of the action; God cannot wish because whatever He wills must either exist or not exist as He wills. In contrast, power is an attribute that necessarily belongs to the agent who possesses it; if God created power for an action, He would have that power Himself, which is impossible to deny. Therefore, creating power for an action without being powerful is impossible, while creating wish is possible because wish is not an attribute of God.

Key Terms
قدرة
تمن
إرادة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Wish and power are similar in that both can be created by God without Him possessing them.
Correction: Wish is not a divine attribute and can exist without the agent being a wisher, whereas power is an attribute that necessarily belongs to the agent; if God creates power for an action, He must have that power.

Checkpoint
Why does the author reject the analogy between God creating wish and God creating power?
A) Because wish is an act of God while power is not.
B) Because power is an attribute of the agent, so if God creates power for an action, He must have that power.
C) Because wish is always coupled with action while power is not.
D) Because the objector's question is based on a false premise.

Key Takeaway
The impossibility of God creating power for an action without being powerful is due to power being an essential attribute of the agent, unlike wish.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you wish for something that does not happen, do you truly submit to God's will, or does your heart harbor resentment? This lesson teaches that God's will is always effective, while wishes may be separated from their objects. Where is your trust placed when your wishes go unfulfilled?
?
The passage shows that God's power is inseparable from His essence. Do you ever act as if you can 'create' power or control in your life independently of God? Where do you need to surrender your illusion of self-sufficiency and acknowledge that all power belongs to Him alone?
?
If God's power is always present and cannot be divorced from Him, then every moment of your life is under His direct control. Does this comfort you or unsettle you? How can you cultivate a deeper awareness of His absolute power in your daily decisions and fears?

Divine Decree

One Action Cannot Be Under Two Creative Powers
Refutation of the view that God and servant each have creative power over the same action.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why attributing creative power to both God and the servant over the same action leads to absurd consequences, specifically the disabling of God.

Core Idea
If the servant creates, God either creates what exists or loses power—both impossible.

This passage tackles a critical issue in Islamic theology: can God and a human both have creative power over the same action? The author systematically refutes this by showing that any scenario leads to contradiction or the disabling of God. As you read, follow the logical steps carefully.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and its translation
First, read the full Arabic passage once and then its literal translation. Get a feel for the argument's structure without analyzing too deeply.
The passage uses technical terms like 'ta'jiz' (disabling).
2
Understand the initial division
The author sets up a division: either one action can be under two powers or not. Since the first is impossible by necessity, the only option is that each agent has independent power over the action.
This is the position of those who affirm creative power for the servant.
3
Analyze the first consequence
Assume the servant creates the action. Then ask: does God retain the power to create it? If yes, that means creating what already exists, which is impossible. If no, then the servant has removed or prevented God's power, which is disabling God—also impossible.
The two outcomes are exhaustively considered.
4
Examine the second objection (recreation)
An opponent might say that God creates the action in a second moment. The author argues this fails: either the action is perishable (and recreating would make it enduring) or enduring (and it already exists). Both are impossible.
This rebuttal closes the loophole.
5
Conclude the argument
The passage ends by asserting that affirming creative power for the servant necessarily leads to disabling God, which is absurd. Therefore, only God has creative power.
This reinforces the doctrine of tawhid in actions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وهذا القسم يليق بالفصل الثالث وهو أن دخول مقدور واحد تحت قدرة قادرين من جملة المجوزات، وإنما وقفنا فيه في هذا الفعل لضرورة التقسيم وإذا بطل هذا القسم تعين القسم الآخر وهو أن الله تعالى عليه قدرة كما أن للعبد عليه قدرة، وينفرد كل واحد بإيجاده كما ذهب إليه طائفة منهم. ثم على هذا القسم نلزم فنقول : إذا أوجد العبد هل بقى الله تعالى على ذلك الفعل قدرة الإيجاد ؟ فإن قالوا: نعم، فقد أحالوا، لأن إيجاد الموجود محال. وإن قالوا: لا، فقد أقروا بإزالة العبد قدرة الله تعالى عما كانت ثابتة عليه ومنعه إياه عن إيجاد ما كان قادرا على إيجاده، وهذا هو التعجيز عندنا والمنع عندكم، وكل ذلك محال ممتنع، والله الموفق. فإن قالوا: يوجده الله تعالى في الحالة الثانية من حالة وجوده الذي أوجده قيل: الإلزام في الحالة الأولى باق ولا اعتراض لكم عليه، ثم إيجاده أيضا في الحالة الثانية مستحيل، فإن ما أوجده العبد عندنا مستحيل البقاء وعندكم على اختلاف فيما بينكم قد يكون مما يبقى وقد يكون مما لا يبقى - فإن كان مما لا يبقى فإيجاده في الثاني مستحيل، لأنه يصير باقيًا لوجوده في الوقتين المتواليين اللذين لا يتصور العدم فيهما بينهما، وهذا هو حد البقاء والقول ببقاء ما لا يبقى محال، وإن كان مما يبقى فهو في الثاني من زمان وجوده موجود وإيجاد الموجود محال. وعرف بهذه الفصول : أن القول بإثبات قدرة التخليق للعبد يؤدي إلى تعجيز الله تعالى، وهو محال
And this section belongs to the third chapter: that one thing capable of being done coming under the power of two agents is among the permissible things. But we stopped concerning this particular action due to the necessity of division. If this section becomes invalid, the other section is determined: that God has power over it, just as the servant has power over it, and each one uniquely brings it into existence, as a group among them held. Then upon this section we oblige and say: When the servant brings into existence, does God retain the power of bringing into existence over that action? If they say: Yes, then they have committed an impossibility, because bringing into existence what already exists is impossible. If they say: No, then they have admitted that the servant removed God's power from what was established upon it and prevented Him from bringing into existence what He was able to bring into existence. This is disabling (ta'jiz) according to us, and prevention according to you, and all that is impossible and prohibited. And God is the provider of success. If they say: God brings it into existence in the second state from the state of its existence which He brought into existence. It was said: The necessitation in the first state remains, and you have no objection against it. Then also bringing it into existence in the second state is impossible. For what the servant brings into existence, according to us, is impossible of endurance; and according to you, with differences among yourselves, it may be that which endures or that which does not endure. If it is that which does not endure, then bringing it into existence in the second is impossible, because it becomes enduring by its existence in two consecutive times between which non-existence is not conceivable. This is the definition of endurance, and saying that what does not endure endures is impossible. If it is that which endures, then in the second of its time of existence it exists, and bringing into existence what exists is impossible. And by these sections it is known that the statement affirming creative power (qudrat al-takhliq) for the servant leads to disabling God, and that is impossible.
Source pages p. 887

Commentary
This passage presents a proof that one action cannot be under the power of two agents independently. First, it sets up the division: either one action is under the power of two agents or not. If it is not, then the only possibility is that each agent has power over the action, but each creates it independently. Then the author examines the consequence: if the servant creates the action, does God retain power of creation over that action? If yes, then bringing into existence what already exists is impossible. If no, then the servant has removed God's power and prevented Him from creating, which is disabling (ta'jiz) of God. Then a second objection: that God recreates the action in a second moment. The answer: the objection remains in the first state. Moreover, recreating is impossible because either the action is perishable or enduring. If perishable, bringing it again amounts to making it enduring, which is impossible. If enduring, it already exists, so bringing it again is impossible. Conclusion: attributing creative power to the servant leads to disabling God, which is absurd.

Key Terms
قدرة
تخليق
تعجيز
مقدور

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That God and the servant can both have creative power over the same action without contradiction.
Correction: The passage shows that if both create the same action, either God creates what already exists (impossible) or the servant removes God's power (disabling God, impossible). Therefore, such shared creative power is impossible.

Checkpoint
According to the author, what follows if the servant is said to have creative power (qudrat al-takhliq) over an action?
A) God shares the same power without contradiction.
B) The servant's power disables God by removing His power or preventing Him from creation.
C) The action becomes impossible to exist.
D) God recreates the action in a second moment.

Key Takeaway
Attributing creative power to the servant necessarily leads to disabling God, which is impossible, so only God can be the Creator.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
When you sin, do you feel as if you 'created' that act entirely independently of God? How does this lesson's demonstration that such a belief leads to disabling God humble you and drive you to seek His forgiveness and guidance?
?
This lesson shows that attributing any independent creative power to the servant is impossible. In what areas of your life do you still subtly act as if you have ultimate control? What would change if you truly internalized that only God creates?

The Impossibility of One Act Under Two Independent Powers
The author divides the possibilities and proves the impossibility of one act under two powers.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Maturidi theologians argue that one act cannot be created independently by both Allah and the servant, and how this preserves the uniqueness of divine creative power.

Core Idea
The same act cannot be independently created by two agents without absurdity.

Imam al-Nasafi now addresses a key question in the debate on human agency: can one and the same act fall under the power of two agents, each creating it independently? This passage presents a logical division and a dilemma that exposes the consequences of affirming such a view. As you read, notice how the author forces the opponent into a contradiction.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic carefully, then the literal translation. Note the logical structure: division, then dilemma on the second division.
The passage begins with 'This section belongs to the third chapter'.
2
Understand the initial division
The author categorizes the possibility of one act under two powers as permissible in itself, but here he stops to examine it due to the necessity of the division. What are the two possible sections?
3
Analyze the dilemma
Focus on the question: when the servant creates, does Allah still have creative power? Trace the two possible answers and their consequences.
The author calls the consequence 'ta'jiz' (incapacitation) and deems it impossible.
4
Connect to broader theology
Reflect on why this argument is crucial: it defends God's omnipotence from being limited by human action.
This is a key Maturid proof against the Mu'tazilite view of independent human creation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وهذا القسم يليق بالفصل الثالث (۱) وهو أن دخول مقدور واحد تحت قدرة قادرين من جملة المجوزات، وإنما وقفنا فيه في هذا الفعل لضرورة التقسيم وإذا بطل هذا القسم تعين (۲) القسم الآخر وهو أن الله تعالى عليه قدرة كما أن للعبد عليه قدرة، وينفرد كل واحد بإيجاده كما ذهب إليه طائفة منهم. ثم على هذا القسم نلزم فنقول : إذا أوجد العبد هل بقى الله تعالى على ذلك الفعل قدرة الإيجاد ؟ فإن قالوا: نعم، فقد أحالوا ، لأن إيجاد الموجود محل. وإن قالوا: لا، فقد أقروا بإزالة العبد قدرة الله تعالى عما كانت ثابتة عليه ومنعه إياه عن إيجاد ما كان قادرا على إيجاده، وهذا هو التعجيز عندنا والمنع عندكم، وكل ذلك محال ممتنع، والله الموفق.
This section belongs to the third chapter (1): namely, that one act falling under the power of two agents is among the permissible things. We stopped at this act only due to the necessity of division. When this section is invalidated, the other section is determined (2): that Allah the Exalted has power over it just as the servant has power over it, and each one creates it independently, as a group among them held. Then on this section we impose [a problem] and say: When the servant creates, does Allah the Exalted retain creative power over that act? If they say: Yes, then they have deemed it impossible, because creating what already exists is impossible. If they say: No, then they have admitted that the servant removes Allah's power from what it was established upon and prevents Him from creating what He was able to create. This is incapacitation (ta'jiz) according to us and prevention according to you. All of that is impossible and impermissible. And Allah is the granter of success.
Source pages p. 887

Commentary
This passage is a logical division and proof. The author begins by stating that the case of one act falling under the power of two agents belongs to the category of permissible possibilities. However, due to the necessity of the division, the author stops at this act. Then he argues: if this division (one act under two powers) is invalidated, the only remaining division is that both Allah and the servant have power over the act, but each creates it independently, as held by some groups. Then, on this second division, the author imposes a dilemma: when the servant creates the act, does Allah still have creative power over it? There are only two possible answers. If they say 'yes', then they have deemed it impossible because creating an already existing thing is absurd. If they say 'no', then they admit that the servant has removed Allah's power from that act and prevented Him from creating what He was able to create. This is 'ta'jiz' (incapacitation) in the author's terminology. Both conclusions are impossible, so the initial position (that both Allah and the servant independently create the same act) is false. The author ends with 'And Allah is the granter of success.'

Misconception: The argument is just a hypothetical exercise without real theological stakes.
Correction: The argument shows that the Mu'tazilite position of independent human creation forces a choice between affirming the impossible (creating the already existing) or denying God's power—both unacceptable.

Checkpoint

A) It is permissible because God delegates power.

C) It shows that God and the servant share power equally.
D) It is impossible because creation cannot be repeated.

Key Takeaway
Affirming independent human creation of the same act leads to the impossible consequence of either creating the already existing or incapacitating God.

Refutation of the objection that God creates in a second state
Author answers objection about God creating in a second state.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational impossibility of the objection that God could create an act in a second state after the servant's initial creation, and why this leads to attributing impotence to Allah.

Core Idea
Creating what already exists is impossible, and recreating the non-persistent would make it persistent.

In this passage, the author tackles a specific objection: could God simply create a second act to evade the problem? The response uses the objector's own assumptions to show that this is impossible. By analyzing two scenarios, the author demonstrates that such a move either contradicts the nature of the act or entails creating an already existing thing. Understanding this argument deepens our grasp of how kalam reasoning refutes theological objections.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
The objector proposes that God creates the act in a second state. Read the first line of the passage to grasp the hypothetical.
2
Follow the two-part refutation
The author gives two cases based on whether the act can persist or not. Identify each case and why it leads to impossibility.
3
Note the definition of persistence
Persistence means existence over two consecutive times with no non-existence in between. The author uses this to show impossibility.
4
Grasp the conclusion
The passage ends with a powerful conclusion: affirming creative power for the servant leads to impossible consequences.
This connects to broader issues of divine omnipotence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قالوا: يوجده الله تعالى في الحالة الثانية من حالة وجوده الذي أوجده قيل: الإلزام في الحالة الأولى باق ولا اعتراض لكم عليه، ثم إيجاده أيضا في الحالة الثانية مستحيل، فإن ما أوجده العبد عندنا مستحيل البقاء وعندكم على اختلاف فيما بينكم قد يكون مما يبقى وقد يكون مما لا يبقى - فإن كان مما لا يبقى فإيجاده في الثاني مستحيل، لأنه يصير باقيًا لوجوده في الوقتين المتواليين اللذين لا يتصور العدم فيهما بينهما، وهذا هو حد البقاء والقول ببقاء ما لا يبقى محال، وإن كان مما يبقى فهو في الثاني من زمان وجوده موجود وإيجاد الموجود محال. وعرف بهذه الفصول : أن القول بإثبات قدرة التخليق (۳) للعبد يؤدي إلى تعجيز الله تعالى، وهو محال
For if they say: Allah creates it in the second state from the state of its existence which He created, it is said: The problem in the first state remains, and you have no objection against it. Then also His creating it in the second state is impossible, for what the servant created is, according to us, impossible to persist, and according to you, with variation among yourselves, it may be that which persists or may be that which does not persist. If it is that which does not persist, then creating it in the second state is impossible, because it would become persistent due to its existence in two consecutive times between which non-existence is inconceivable, and this is the definition of persistence, and saying that that which does not persist persists is impossible. And if it is that which persists, then it exists in the second time of its existence, and creating what exists is impossible. And it is known through these sections that the statement affirming creative power (qudrat al-takhlīq) (3) for the servant leads to incapacitation of Allah the Exalted, and that is impossible.
Source pages p. 887

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer pair. The objectors propose: what if God creates the act in a second state after the servant's initial creation? The author replies with a two-part refutation. First, the original problem (the first state) remains untouched. Second, creating in the second state is impossible for two reasons, depending on whether the servant's act can persist (according to the opponent's own views). If the act cannot persist (as the author holds), then creating it again would make it persistent, which contradicts its nature; this is the definition of persistence as existence over two consecutive times with no non-existence between them. If the act can persist (as some opponents hold), then it already exists in the second moment, so creating it would be creating an existing thing, which is impossible. The conclusion is a summary: all these considerations show that affirming creative power for the servant necessarily leads to the impossibility of God's omnipotence (ta'jiz).

Key Terms

إيجاد
بقاء
تعجيز
تخليق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the objection offers a way out, but the author shows it actually reinforces the initial dilemma.
Correction: The objection does not solve the problem; it leads to logical contradictions that affirm God's absolute power.

Checkpoint

A) Because non-persistent acts cannot be created again.
B) Because it would become persistent, which is contradictory.
C) Because the second state does not exist.
D) Because the act would be eternal.

Key Takeaway
The objection that God could create a second act does not solve the problem; it leads to logical contradictions that affirm God's absolute power.

Al-Ka'bi's Objection on Human Action and the Maturidi Response
Al-Nasafi presents and refutes al-Ka'bi's objection about human action resembling God's.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Ka'bi's objection that if opponents claim their actions resemble God's, they are inconsistent, and how al-Nasafi refutes it by distinguishing between creation and acquisition and showing the objection only applies to those who claim to create like God.

Core Idea
Not all who affirm human action claim to create like God—al-Ka'bi's objection misfires against those who distinguish creation from acquisition.

Al-Ka'bi, a Mu'tazilite, raises a clever objection: if you claim your actions resemble God's but admit they are not identical, you are more guilty of comparison. Al-Nasafi responds by showing that this argument only applies to those who say humans create, like the Mu'tazila themselves, not to the Ash'ariyya or those who affirm acquisition (kasb). This lesson unpacks that exchange.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read al-Ka'bi's objection in the passage. Notice he targets those who say they do like God's action but not identical, accusing them of inconsistency.
The objection is subtle: it tries to force the opponent into claiming their action is exactly like God's.
2
Identify the two groups in the response
Al-Nasafi's response distinguishes between the Ash'ariyya (and Abu Isa al-Barghuth) and the Najjariyya/traditionists. Note how each group defines action differently.
The Ash'ariyya equate action with effect, denying the servant is an agent; the Najjariyya say action is acquisition of what God creates.
3
Understand why the objection fails for each group
For the Ash'ariyya, since they deny the servant is an agent, al-Ka'bi's charge of 'doing like God' doesn't apply. For the Najjariyya, their action is acquisition, not creation, so they don't resemble God's creation.
The objection only sticks to those who claim to create like God, i.e., the Mu'tazila themselves.
4
Reflect on the broader point
This passage shows the Maturidi method of precise distinction-refuting an objection by showing it rebounds on the objector.
This is a classic kalam technique of ilzam (turning the tables).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وهذا اعتراض الكعبي ذكر في المقالات فإن قالوا إنا إن قلنا: إنا نفعل مثل فعل ربنا، ولكن لا شك أن فعلنا غير فعل ربنا وإنكم تزعمون أنكم تفعلون عين فعل ربكم فأنتم أحق بالقول باشتباه فعلكم بفعل ربكم. قلنا لهم : أما الأشعرية وأبو عيسى البرغوث من النجارية فإنهم إن زعموا أن الفعل عين المفعول فإنهم يأبون أن يكون العبد فاعلاً فلا يستقيم قولكم في حقهم إنكم تفعلون فعل ربكم، فسقط كلامكم في حقهم. ومن قال من النجارية ومتكلمي أهل الحديث : أن العبد يفعل إلا أن فعله الاكتساب لما أحدثه الله تعالى لا الإحداث فهؤلاء وإن قرروا بالفعل ولكن فعلهم ليس بخلق فلم يكونوا خلقوا كخلق الله تعالى وأنتم الخالقون بزعمكم كخلق الله تعالى؛ فلزمكم هذا الكلام وسقط عنهم.
And this is the objection of al-Ka'bi mentioned in the articles: they said, 'If we say: we do like the action of our Lord, but there is no doubt that our action is other than the action of our Lord, and you claim that you do the very action of your Lord, then you are more deserving of saying that your action resembles His action.' We said to them: 'As for the Ash'ariyya and Abu Isa al-Barghuth from the Najjariyya, if they claim that the action is identical to the effect, then they refuse that the servant be an agent, so your statement does not apply to them—that you do the action of your Lord—so your argument falls against them. And those among the Najjariyya and the speculative theologians of the people of hadith who say that the servant does act, but his action is acquisition (iktisab) of what God creates, not origination—these, though they affirm action, their action is not creation, so they do not create like God's creation, whereas you claim to create like God's creation; thus this argument applies to you and falls from them.'
Source pages p. 897

Commentary
This passage presents an objection from al-Ka'bi (a Mu'tazilite) and al-Nasafi's response. The objection is: if you (the opponent of the author) say you do like God's action, but you admit your action is not identical to God's, then you are more deserving of the charge of resemblance. Al-Nasafi responds by distinguishing between two groups: the Ash'ariyya and Abu Isa al-Barghuth (a Najjari) hold that action is identical to the effect, so they deny that the servant is an agent, thus the objection does not apply to them. The other group (Najjariyya and speculative traditionists) say the servant's action is acquisition (kasb) of what God creates, not origination—so their action is not creation, whereas the objectors themselves claim to create, so the argument only applies to them.

Key Terms

خلق
اكتساب
الأشعرية
النجارية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Al-Nasafi concedes that humans create actions like God creates.
Correction: Al-Nasafi explicitly distinguishes creation (khalq) from acquisition (kasb); human action is acquisition of what God creates, not origination. The passage shows that only the Mu'tazila claim to create like God.

Checkpoint
According to al-Nasafi, why does al-Ka'bi's objection not apply to the Ash'ariyya?
A) Because the Ash'ariyya affirm that the servant is the agent.
B) Because the Ash'ariyya hold that action is identical to the effect and deny the servant is an agent.
C) Because the Ash'ariyya claim to create like God.
D) Because the Ash'ariyya deny that God creates actions.

Key Takeaway
Al-Ka'bi's objection is countered by showing that the Mu'tazila themselves are the ones who claim to create like God, while others affirm human action without claiming creation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The Maturidi Doctrine: Human Action as Created and Acquired
Al-Nasafi defines the Maturidi position on human action as created by God and acquired by the servant.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Maturidi position that human actions are created by God and acquired by the servant, and why this differs from both the Jabri and Qadari views.

Core Idea
Human actions are God's creation but the servant's acquisition.

Al-Nasafi presents the Maturidi position on human action, a key point of contention in Islamic theology. He carefully distinguishes between God's creative act and the servant's acquisition. This passage clarifies that while God brings all actions into existence, the servant still performs and acquires them, without possessing any creative power of their own.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the precise wording: 'makhluq' and 'maf'ul' vs. 'fi'l' and 'khalq'.
2
Identify the key distinction
The passage distinguishes between God's action (fi'l) which is an eternal attribute, and the servant's action which is God's effect (maf'ul).
This distinction is central to understanding Maturidi theology.
3
Understand the concept of iktisab
The servant 'acquires' (iktisab) the action and 'performs' it (bashara). Note that acquisition does not imply creation.
Iktisab is a technical term meaning the servant's role in the action.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
The passage ends with a rhetorical question emphasizing that the servant has no creation at all. Reflect on why this matters for theological debates on free will.
This guards against the misconception that Maturidis believe in human creative power.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وعندنا فعل العبد هو مخلوق الله ومفعوله لا فعله وخلقه إذ فعل الله تعالى هو الصفة الأزلية القائمة بذاته، وما هو فعل العبد فهو مفعول الله تعالى، والله تعالى هو الذي تولى إيجاده وإخراجه من العدم إلى الوجود، والعبد اكتسبه وباشره فلم يكن فعل العبد مثل فعله ولا خلقه كخلقه، وكيف يكون كذلك ولا خلق للعبد البتة.
And according to us, the action of the servant is the creation of God and His effect, not His action and not His creation, because the action of God is the eternal attribute subsisting in His essence, and what is the action of the servant is the effect of God, and God is the one who undertook its bringing into existence and bringing it out from non-existence to existence, and the servant acquired it and performed it. So the action of the servant is not like His action, nor its creation like His creation. How could it be so, when there is no creation for the servant at all?
Source pages p. 897

Commentary
This passage defines the Maturidi position on human action. The author states that the servant's action is God's creation (makhluq) and God's effect (maf'ul), not God's action (fi'l) nor God's creation (khalq). This is because God's action is an eternal attribute subsisting in His essence, whereas the servant's action is God's effect—God brings it from non-existence to existence. The servant acquires (iktisab) the action and performs it (bashara). Therefore, the servant's action is not like God's action, nor is it creation like God's creation, as the servant has no creative power at all.

Key Terms
فعل العبد
مخلوق
مفعول
صفة أزلية
اكتساب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Maturidi view that the servant acquires actions means the servant also creates them.
Correction: The passage explicitly states the servant has no creation at all; acquisition is non-creative performance.

Checkpoint
According to al-Nasafi, what is the relationship between the servant's action and God's action?
A) The servant's action is identical to God's action.
B) The servant's action is God's creation and effect, not His action.
C) The servant creates his own action independently.
D) The servant's action is an eternal attribute.

Key Takeaway
In Maturidi theology, human actions are entirely created by God yet genuinely acquired by the servant, who has no independent creative power.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?

?

Similarity Between Two Actions: Refuting the Opponents' Logic
Al-Nasafi critiques the similarity claim of opponents.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why al-Nasafi argues that the opponents' claim of similarity between God's action and the servant's action leads to the contradiction that the servant creates like God.

Core Idea
Similarity requires two distinct things; claiming it while denying identity is contradictory.

Al-Nasafi begins this passage by exposing a fundamental flaw in the opponents' reasoning. They assert that the servant's action is other than God's action, yet they also claim the two actions are similar. This leads to the absurd consequence that the servant's action is 'creation like God's creation.'

Guided Path
1
Identify the key flaw
Read the Arabic passage and find the phrase 'الاشتباه يجري بين شيئين' (similarity occurs between two things). Understand that this establishes the logical basis for al-Nasafi's critique.
Similarity requires two distinct entities.
2
Trace the opponent's position
Note that the opponents claim the servant's action is other than God's action, yet they acknowledge the two movements (servant's and God's) are similar.
This is the contradiction al-Nasafi exploits.
3
See the absurd conclusion
Al-Nasafi concludes that, according to the opponents' own logic, there exists 'creation like God's creation' — a claim they would reject.
This shows the flaw in their reasoning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومما يكشف عن عوار هذا الكلام أن الاشتباه يجري بين شيئين، وعندكم فعلكم غير فعل الله تعالى فكانت إحدى الحركتين عندكم فعل العبد والأخرى فعل الله تعالى وهما متشابهتان، فوجد منكم على زعمكم خلق كخلق الله تعالى، فأما عندنا من يسلم لكم من النجارية ومتكلمي أهل الحديث أن العبد له فعل ففعله عندهم عين فعل الله تعالى، فهو من حيث إنه خلق فعل الله تعالى ومن حيث إنه حركة أو سكون طاعة أو معصية فعل العبد، فعين الكسب هو عين الخلق، وعين الخلق هو عين الكسب، كما أن عين الحركة هو عين الشيء وعين الشيء هو عين الحركة وإثبات الاشتباه في شيء واحد محال إذ الشيء لا يشبه نفسه، فكان إلزامكم إياهم فاسد. فعل الله خلق وإيجاد وفعل العبد مباشرة واكتساب. وعندنا فعل العبد وإن كان غير فعل الله إلا أن فعل الله تعالى خلق وإيجاد وفعلنا مباشرة واكتساب فلم يلزم التشابه، والله الموفق.
And among what reveals the flaw of this speech is that similarity occurs between two things, and according to you your action is other than God's action, so one of the two movements according to you is the action of the servant and the other is the action of God, and they are similar—so according to your claim, there exists creation like God's creation. As for according to us, those who concede to you from the Najjariyya and the speculative theologians of the people of hadith that the servant has action—their action is identical to God's action, so it is from the aspect that it is creation, God's action, and from the aspect that it is movement or stillness, obedience or disobedience, the action of the servant. So the identical of acquisition is the identical of creation, and the identical of creation is the identical of acquisition, just as the identical of movement is the identical of the thing, and the identical of the thing is the identical of movement. And establishing similarity in one thing is impossible, because a thing does not resemble itself. So your imputation against them is invalid. God's action is creation and bringing into existence, and the servant's action is direct performance and acquisition. And according to us, the action of the servant, though other than God's action, yet God's action is creation and bringing into existence, and our action is direct performance and acquisition, so similarity does not follow. And God is the one who grants success.
Source pages p. 898

Commentary
This passage continues the refutation by examining the concept of similarity (ishtibah). The author notes that similarity occurs between two distinct things. The opponents claim their action is other than God's action, yet they say the two movements (the servant's and God's) are similar, leading to the claim that they create like God's creation. Al-Nasafi then examines the position of the Najjariyya and traditionist theologians who affirm the servant's action as identical (ayn) to God's action—so the action has a dual aspect: as creation it is God's action, as movement/obedience it is the servant's action. Thus, kasb and creation are identical in essence, like the identity of movement and the thing moved. But if they are identical, then claiming similarity between them is impossible, because a thing does not resemble itself. So the opponent's charge of similarity fails. Al-Nasafi then restates the Maturidi view: God's action is creation and origination, while the servant's action is direct performance and acquisition; they are different in nature, so no similarity arises.

Key Terms
اشتباه
خلق
حركة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the servant's action can be both other than God's and similar to it without contradiction.
Correction: Al-Nasafi shows that this leads to the claim of independent creation, which the opponents reject.

Checkpoint
According to al-Nasafi, what does the opponents' claim of similarity lead to?
A) The servant's action is identical to God's.
B) The servant creates like God creates.
C) The servant's action is non-existent.
D) Similarity is impossible between movements.

Key Takeaway
The opponents' position that the servant's action is both other than God's and similar to it leads to the absurdity that the servant creates like God.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you perform a good deed, do you sincerely attribute it to God's grace or do you feel a sense of personal ownership that implies you are a co-creator with Him? How does this passage expose the inconsistency in taking credit while claiming your action is distinct from God's?
?
If your actions are 'similar' to God's commands but not identical in origin, what responsibility do you bear for aligning your will with His? Are you willing to accept that every movement you make is either from Him or from you—and that claiming similarity while denying His sovereignty is a logical and spiritual contradiction?
?
Consider a sin you commit repeatedly. Do you blame your environment or your nature, saying 'that's just how I am' (as if your action is distinct from God's decree), yet also feel guilty (as if your action is truly yours)? How does Al-Nasafi's argument challenge you to reconcile these two claims in your own heart?

The Identity of Kasb and Creation and the Impossibility of Self-Resemblance
Al-Nasafi analyzes identity and refutes opponent's charge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Nasafi refutes the opponent's imputation by showing that the identity of kasb and creation leads to self-resemblance, which is impossible, and then affirms the Maturidi distinction.

Core Idea
A thing cannot resemble itself; thus claiming similarity in one thing is invalid.

In the second part of the passage, al-Nasafi examines the position of the Najjariyya and traditionist theologians who affirm the servant's action as identical (ayn) to God's action. He shows that this identity makes the opponent's charge of similarity impossible because it would be self-resemblance. He then restates the Maturidi view as a coherent alternative.

Guided Path
1
Understand the identity claim
Read the part about the Najjariyya: the servant's action is 'ayn (identical to) God's action. This means the same entity has two aspects: as creation it is God's, as acquisition it is the servant's.
The term 'عين' (ayn) means identical essence.
2
Follow the analogy of movement
Al-Nasafi gives an analogy: 'the identical of movement is the identical of the thing.' This illustrates how two descriptions can apply to the same reality.
Use the analogy to grasp the concept.
3
See the refutation of the opponent
Because the action is identical to God's, claiming similarity between them would be like a thing resembling itself, which is impossible. Therefore, the opponent's imputation is invalid.
Self-resemblance is logically absurd.
4
Learn the Maturidi resolution
The passage concludes with the Maturidi distinction: God's action is creation (khalq) and bringing into existence (ijad), while the servant's action is direct performance (mubashara) and acquisition (iktisab). They are different in nature, so no similarity arises.
This avoids the contradictions of the other views.

The Two Sticks Analogy: Refuting Distinction of Actions by Cause
Al-Nasafi uses the two sticks example to refute distinction by cause.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Nasafi uses the two sticks analogy to refute the claim that human and divine actions can be distinguished by their observable cause.

Core Idea
If the cause is unseen, the distinction fails—the two sticks prove it.

This lesson presents al-Nasafi's powerful refutation of the argument that actions can be distinguished by looking at their cause. Using a simple analogy of two sticks protruding from behind a wall, one moved by a human and the other by God, he shows that when the cause is invisible, no distinction is possible. This challenges opponents who rely on observable causality.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without pausing. Then read the English translation to grasp the overall argument.
Notice the dialogue format: the opponent raises an objection, and al-Nasafi responds.
2
Identify the opponent's argument and al-Nasafi's counter
The opponent claims that looking behind the wall to see the cause distinguishes the movements. Al-Nasafi counters by asking what happens if the cause is an invisible spirit.
This reveals the flaw: if the cause is unseen, the distinction collapses.
3
Reflect on the implication
Ask yourself why this analogy is considered a 'demolisher' of the opponent's position. Consider how it undermines any attempt to distinguish actions by their cause when the cause is not perceptible.
This refutation is decisive in the debate on human vs. divine agency.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والذي يهدم جميع كلامه فصل القصبتين وهو أن قصبتين ظهرت أعاليهما من وراء جدار ، يحرك أحديهما العبد والأخرى يحركها الله تعالى، فعاينهما إنسان لن يفصل البتة بين الحركة التي خلقها الله تعالى وبين الحركة التي خلقها العبد لاستوائهما في ذاتيهما من جميع الوجوه. فإن قالوا: يمكن الفصل بين الحركتين بالرجوع إلى السبب، فإنا ننظر وراء الجدار فإذا رأينا إنسانا يحرك أحديهما ولم نر محركًا من البشر للآخر علمنا أن الأولى فعل الخلق والثانية فعل الله تعالى. قلنا لهم أرأيتم لو أن المحرك لأحديهما كان هو الله تعالى والمحرك الآخر بعض الأرواح الناطقة من الملائكة أو الجن أو الشياطين الذين لم تجر المادة برؤيتنا إياهم وعندكم يستحيل رؤيته للطافته، فبم تفصلون بينهما؟
And what demolishes all his speech is the chapter of the two sticks: it is that two sticks, their tops appear from behind a wall—one of them is moved by the servant, and the other is moved by God. So a person who sees them will never be able to distinguish between the movement created by God and the movement created by the servant, because they are equal in their essences from all aspects. If they say: 'It is possible to distinguish between the two movements by returning to the cause; we look behind the wall—if we see a human moving one of them, and we do not see a human mover for the other, we know that the first is the action of the creature and the second is the action of God.' We said to them: 'Consider if the mover of one of them was God, and the mover of the other was some rational spirit from the angels, jinn, or devils, whose matter does not permit us to see them, and according to you it is impossible to see them due to their subtlety—how would you distinguish between them?'
Source pages p. 898

Commentary
This passage presents a conclusive refutation using the analogy of two sticks protruding from behind a wall. One stick is moved by a human, the other by God. An observer cannot distinguish between the movements because they are identical in essence. The opponent objects that one can look behind the wall to see the cause: if a human mover is seen, it is human action; if no human mover is seen, it is God's action. Al-Nasafi counters by imagining that one mover is God and the other is an invisible spirit (angel, jinn, or devil) that cannot be seen. Since according to the opponent's own principles, such spirits are imperceptible, they would be unable to tell which movement is caused by which. Thus, the distinction by cause fails when the cause is unseen, showing that the opponent's argument is unsound.

Key Terms
قصبتين
حركة
سبب
ملائكة
جن

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the two movements are inherently distinguishable.
Correction: The passage shows they are identical in essence and only distinguishable by cause, but this fails when the cause is unseen (e.g., an invisible spirit).

Checkpoint
What does al-Nasafi use to refute the opponent's claim that actions can be distinguished by their cause?
A) The two sticks example, where one mover is God and the other is a human.
B) The two sticks example, where the mover of one stick is God and the other is an invisible spirit.
C) The argument that human actions are always visible.
D) The impossibility of God moving objects.

Key Takeaway
The two sticks analogy shows that if the cause of an action is unknown, actions cannot be distinguished, undermining the opponent's reliance on observable causality.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you experience a blessing or trial, do you instinctively attribute it to a visible cause (your effort, another person) rather than to God's direct action? How does the two sticks analogy challenge you to reconsider that habit?
?
If you cannot always see the true cause behind events, how does that affect your trust in God's wisdom and justice, especially when things don't go as you planned?
?
Think of a time you felt proud of your own accomplishment. Could the same outcome have been from God through an unseen agent? How does that realization humble you and shift your gratitude to Him alone?

First Objection on Act and Creation Refuted
Refutation of objection that act implies creation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the refutation of the objection that a servant's act implies he is its creator.

Core Idea
Having an act does not require having the power of creation.

The opponents argue that if the servant performs an action, that action must be created by him. Imam al-Nasafi refutes this by reminding that the proof has already established that creation belongs only to Allah, leaving no room for doubt.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the first objection which claims that affirming an act for the servant means he is the inventor of it.
Notice how the opponents try to link act with creation.
2
Study the refutation
Read the author's response: the proof has already shown that creative power is exclusive to Allah.
The refutation does not repeat the proof but appeals to it.
3
Reflect on the finality
The author says the proof leaves no room for doubt, indicating certainty.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قالوا: إذا ثبت أن العبد له فعل ثبت أنه هو المخترع له. قلنا: قد أقمنا الدلالة على أن ليس لغير الله تعالى قدرة الاختراع على وجه لم يبق للشك فيه مجال. فإن قالوا : لو لم يكن للعبد قدرة الاختراع لم يكن له فعل، وكان مضطرًا فيما يحصل منه من الأفعال. قلنا قد أقمنا الدلالة على أن له فعلاً يعرف بطريق الضرورة الفرق بين الأفعال الضرورية وبين الأفعال الاختيارية.
They say: If it is established that the servant has an act, then it is established that he is the inventor of it. We say: We have established the proof that there is no capacity for invention for other than God Almighty in a manner that leaves no room for doubt. Then they say: If the servant does not have the capacity for invention, then he has no act, and he would be compelled in what occurs of actions from him. We say: We have established the proof that he has an act known by the way of necessity, the distinction between necessary actions and voluntary actions.
Source pages p. 900

Commentary
This passage presents two objections from the opponents and their refutations. The first objection argues that if the servant has an act, then he must be the creator (mukhtari') of that act. The author replies that the proof has already been established that the capacity for creation (ikhtira') belongs only to Allah, leaving no room for doubt. The second objection argues that if the servant lacks creative power, then he has no real act and is merely compelled (mudtarr) in his actions. The author replies that the proof has already been established that the servant does have an act, and this is known by necessary knowledge (darurah) through the distinction between involuntary actions (af'al daruriyyah) and voluntary actions (af'al ikhtiyariyyah). Thus, the servant's act is affirmed without attributing creation to him.

Key Terms
قدرة الاختراع
فعل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that if a person performs an action, they must be the creator of that action.
Correction: The author clarifies that having an act is not the same as having the power of invention; only Allah creates.

Checkpoint
What is the basis of the author's refutation of the first objection?
A) The servant has no act at all.
B) The proof that only Allah has creative power is already established decisively.
C) The act is not real but metaphorical.
D) The objection is irrational.

Key Takeaway
Affirming an act for the servant does not imply he is its creator; creation belongs solely to Allah.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you do a good deed, do you ever feel pride as if you created that goodness yourself? How does this lesson remind you that every good act is from Allah, and you are merely the locus of His action? What would it mean for you to truly internalize that you are not the creator of your own obedience?
?
Think of a sin you struggle with. Do you fall into the trap of thinking you are the sole cause of your transgression, leading to despair? This lesson teaches that Allah alone creates actions. How can this truth free you from both arrogance and hopelessness, and drive you to rely solely on Him?
?
If only Allah has the power to invent, then your very existence and every act of yours is entirely dependent on Him. When you pray, do you truly feel that it is Allah who creates your prayer? How does this impact your khushu' and your sense of reliance? Commit today to whisper before each action: 'Ya Allah, create this act in me.'

Second Objection on Act and Compulsion Refuted
Refutation of objection that no creative power means no act.

Learning Objective

Core Idea
Voluntary actions prove the servant has an act without needing creative power.

The opponents further argue that if the servant lacks creative power, he cannot have any real act and is merely compelled. Imam al-Nasafi responds by affirming that the servant does have an act, and this is known by necessary knowledge through the clear distinction between involuntary and voluntary actions.

Guided Path
1
Read the second objection
The opponents claim that without creative power, the servant has no act and is compelled in his actions.
This objection tries to deny the reality of human agency.
2
Study the refutation
The author says the act is known by necessity (darurah) through the distinction between necessary and voluntary actions.
Necessary actions are those beyond control, like blinking; voluntary actions are chosen.
3
Reflect on the argument
The distinction itself proves the reality of the servant's act without attributing creation to him.

3 min
Refuting the Objection on Capacity's Relation to Origination
Author answers an objection about qudra's relation to ibda'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author refutes the objection that the capacity (qudra) can only relate to origination (ibda') by redirecting the objection to the already established proof of the impossibility of creation by other than Allah.

Core Idea
The author does not explain the relation; he points to proven impossibility.

This final passage from the section on capacity deals with an objection that the capacity (qudra) of the servant can only be related to origination (ibda'), and the manner of that relation is inconceivable. The author does not try to elaborate on the relation; instead, he reminds that the proof for the impossibility of creation by other than Allah has already been given. Hence, any objection should target that proof.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing it. Then read the literal translation to grasp the objection and the author's response.
Pay attention to the rhetorical structure: they say... then we say...
2
Identify the objection
The opponents argue that qudra relates only to ibda', and that the mode of relation is inconceivable. Recognize what they are trying to prove: that the servant cannot have real qudra.
3
Analyze the author's response
The author does not explain the relation. Instead, he says: 'We have already established that ibda' and ikhtira' are impossible for other than Allah.' So he redirects the objection to that proof.
This is a common dialectical move: if the opponent's objection relies on a premise you have already refuted, you can point that out.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبهذا يجابون عن قولهم : إن لا تعلق للقدرة إلا بالإحداث وتعلقها بوجه الإحداث غير معقول. فإنا نقول لهم : قد أقمنا الدلالة على استحالة ثبوت قدرة الاختراع والإحداث لغير الله تعالى فعليكم أن تعترضوا على ذلك الدليل.

Commentary
This passage presents a final objection and its answer. The opponents argue that the capacity (qudra) of the servant can only be related to ibda' (origination), but the manner of that relation is inconceivable. Therefore, the servant cannot have a real capacity. The author responds by reiterating that the proof has already been established that ibda' and ikhtira' are impossible for other than Allah. Thus, the opponents should direct their objection to that proof itself, rather than asking how the capacity relates to ibda'.

The Maturidi Defense of Kasb: Power and the Servant's Act
Refutes objections about divine and human power, explaining the servant's act as acquired not invented.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidis defend the concept of kasb (acquisition) by distinguishing between invention (ikhtirāʿ) and the relation of power to an act.

Core Idea
The servant’s act is real but not invented by him; God creates it and the servant acquires it.

This passage tackles a sharp objection: if power only relates to bringing something into existence, how can human action be meaningful? The Maturidi answer hinges on a careful distinction between creation and acquisition, and it defends the reality of human responsibility without compromising divine omnipotence.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and initial response
Start by reading the first objection: 'Power has no relation except to bringing into existence.' Then note how the author responds by referencing a prior proof that only God can invent.
The author does not repeat the proof; he references it.
2
Follow the second objection and reply
The second objection claims that if invention is impossible for the servant, then his power has no object. The author replies with a second proof that the servant indeed has an act that is within his power (maqdūr).
Notice the phrase 'by the combination of the two proofs'.
3
Understand the Maturidi elaboration
Read the final part where the author distinguishes between what God creates in beings without choice vs. beings with choice. For the servant, the act becomes his through his choice and power, though God is the inventor.
This is the doctrine of kasb: acquisition without invention.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبهذا يجابون عن قولهم : إن لا تعلق للقدرة إلا بالإحداث وتعلقها بوجه الإحداث غير معقول. فإنا نقول لهم : قد أقمنا الدلالة على استحالة ثبوت قدرة الاختراع والإحداث لغير الله تعالى فعليكم أن تعترضوا على ذلك الدليل. فإن قالوا: لو استحال ذلك لبطل تعلق قدرة العبد بشيء وتعطلت قدرته. قلنا : وقد أقمنا الدليل على أن العبد له فعل هو مقدوره فجيب الانقياد للدليل وإثبات الفعل للعبد وإن لم يخترعه، وثبت بمجموع الدليلين جواز تعلق القدرة لا بجهة الاختراع. ثم نقول : ما يخترعه الله تعالى فيما لا اختيار له لا يكون فعلاً له فبكون مخلوق الله تعالى ومفعوله لا فعله ولا فعل غيره عندنا، وعند الأشعرية والنجارية يكون فعل الله تعالى لأنه مفعوله والفعل والمعفول واحد، وما يخترعه فيمن له الاختيار، إن كان ذلك الشيء مما لا يتعلق به قدرة المحل فهو أيضا ليس بفعل للعبد بل العبد محله لا غير، وما يخترعه فيه باختيار العبد ذلك وله عليه قدرة فأثر تعلق قدرته كونه فعلاً، فيكون الله تعالى مخترعا فعل العبد باختياره لولا اختيار العبد وقصده اكتسابه لما خلقه الله تعالى فعلا له فأثر قدرته كون ذلك المخترع فعلاً له، هذا عن جهة التبرع مع أنا لا حاجة بنا إلى بيان ذلك على ما بينا من أن وجوب اتباع الدليلين يقتضي ما قلنا، والله الموفق.
And by this they are answered regarding their saying: 'Power (al-qudra) has no relation except to bringing into existence (al-iḥdāth), and its relation to the aspect of bringing into existence is not intelligible.' For we say to them: We have established the proof (dalāla) of the impossibility of establishing a power of invention (ikhtirāʿ) and bringing into existence for other than God Almighty, so you must object to that proof. If they say: 'If that is impossible, then the relation of the servant's power to anything would be nullified and his power would be rendered inoperative.' We say: And we have established the proof that the servant has an act (fiʿl) that is his power (maqdūr), so one must submit to the proof and affirm the act for the servant even if he did not invent it (yakhṭariʿuh). And it is established by the combination of the two proofs the permissibility of the relation of power not by way of invention (ikhtirāʿ). Then we say: What God Almighty invents in that which has no choice is not an act for it, so it is a creation (makhluq) of God and a made thing (mafʿūl), not an act of it nor an act of another according to us. According to the Ashʿariyya and the Najjāriyya, it is an act of God because it is His made thing, and the act and the made are one. And what He invents in one who has choice, if that thing is such that the power of the locus (al-maḥall) does not relate to it, then it is also not an act of the servant; rather, the servant is its locus only. And what He invents in it by the choice of the servant and the servant has power over it, then the effect of the relation of his power is that it becomes an act. So God Almighty is the inventor of the servant's act by His choice; were it not for the servant's choice and his intention of acquisition (iktisāb), God would not have created it as an act for him. So the effect of his power is that that invented thing becomes an act for him. This is by way of generosity (tabarruʿ), even though we have no need to explain that, given what we have explained that the obligation of following the two proofs necessitates what we say. And God is the granter of success.
Source pages p. 901

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer unit within the Māturīdī discussion of power and human action. The opponents first object: 'Power (qudra) only relates to bringing into existence (iḥdāth), and its relation to that aspect is unintelligible.' The author replies that they have already proven that no one other than God can have the power of invention (ikhtirāʿ). The opponents then ask: 'If that is impossible, then the servant's power would have no object and would be useless.' The author responds that they have also proven that the servant has an act that is within his power, so one must accept that the servant has an act even if he does not invent it. The combination of the two proofs shows that power can relate to something without being inventive. The author then elaborates on the Māturīdī position: what God creates in something without choice (e.g., inanimate objects) is not an act of that thing; it is simply God's creation. What God creates in a being with choice (e.g., humans) can be an act of that being if the being chooses it and has power over it. The act is the servant's through acquisition (kasb), while God is the true originator (mukhtariʿ). The author notes that this explanation is a kindness, since the obligation to follow the two proofs already suffices. The passage defends the Māturīdī doctrine of kasb (acquisition) against opponents who argue that power can only relate to direct creation.

Key Terms
قدرة (qudra)
إحداث (iḥdāth)
اختراع (ikhtirāʿ)
فعل العبد (fiʿl al-ʿabd)
مقدور (maqdūr)
اكتساب (iktisāb)
محل (maḥall)
أشعرية (Ashʿariyya)
نجارية (Najjāriyya)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The servant must invent his own act for it to be truly his act.
Correction: The passage explains that the servant's act is real and belongs to him through acquisition (kasb), while God is the sole inventor. The servant does not need to invent; his choice and power make the divinely created act his.

Checkpoint
According to the Māturīdī position in this passage, what makes an act belong to the servant?
A) The servant invents the act.
B) The servant chooses and has power over it, and God creates it.
C) The servant performs the act without any divine involvement.
D) The act is identical to God's creation.

Key Takeaway
The Maturidi doctrine of kasb affirms human responsibility while maintaining God as the sole creator of all acts.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly believe that every action you perform is created by God and yet you are held accountable? Does that realization make you more careful in your intentions, knowing that without your choice, God would not create that act as yours? What will you change today in how you approach your daily decisions?
?
The passage says the servant's power makes the act 'his act.' Does your heart feel the weight of that responsibility? Think of one habit you justify as 'just the way I am' – can you now see it as an act you acquire, and therefore can change with God's help? What step will you take right now to realign that habit with obedience?
?
If you truly accepted that you do not invent your own deeds, would you be more humble in success and more repentant in failure? Consider a recent good deed: did you secretly feel you 'created' it? How can you train your heart to attribute all good to God while still striving with your full capacity?
