كسب
معتزلة
معدوم
شيئية
قدرة
تعطيل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Mu'tazila think kasb is irrational because it is not perceptible.
Correction: Nasafi argues that kasb is rational even if not perceptible, and that the Mu'tazila's own belief (non-existent as a thing) is what is truly irrational.

Checkpoint
What consequence does Nasafi draw from the Mu'tazila belief that the non-existent is a thing?
A) It proves the reality of human free will.
B) It leads to the nullification of the Creator and the eternity of the world.
C) It proves that kasb is irrational.
D) It establishes that God creates all actions directly.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila's own doctrine of the non-existent leads to absurd theological consequences, proving their claim of irrationality against kasb is baseless.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hold a theological position, do you hold it because it is the view of your group or because you have examined the proofs? Are you willing to abandon partisanship like Nasafi calls for, even if it means changing a deeply held belief?
?
Nasafi warns that the Mu'tazila's error leads to nullifying the Creator and His commands. Do you treat theological errors as mere intellectual differences, or do you recognize that wrong beliefs about Allah can spiritually devastate you? Have you ever avoided questioning a belief because it felt comfortable?
?
If you were to apply Nasafi's method of 'looking with intellect and submitting to the proof' to your own understanding of qadar, what would you need to re-examine? Are you ready to follow the truth wherever it leads, even if it means admitting you were wrong?

The Correct Doctrine of Kasb: Divine and Human Agency
Nasafi affirms the correct doctrine of kasb by dividing agency between God and servant.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the correct Sunni position on kasb: that God creates existence and thingness, while the servant's power contributes to the specific qualities of actions.

Core Idea
God creates existence; the servant's kasb determines the moral character of the action.

After refuting the Mu'tazila objection, Nasafi presents the correct Islamic position on human action. He distinguishes between the existence and thingness of an action, which come from God, and the specific attributes like motion and obedience, which are related to the servant's power. This lesson explains that nuanced division of agency.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage where Nasafi states the correct view. Notice the two parts: God creates all things, and for human actions, there is a division.
The passage contrasts the Sunni view with the Mu'tazila view.
2
Identify the two aspects of action
Find where Nasafi says the existence and thingness of an action are from God, while the qualities (motion/rest, obedience/disobedience) are from the servant.
This division is key to understanding kasb.
3
Contrast with Mu'tazila view
Note that the Mu'tazila deny that thingness is related to any power. Compare this with the Sunni view where God's power relates to thingness.
This highlights the rational coherence of kasb.

?

Is the Dispute with Mu'tazila Merely Semantic?
Al-Nasafi argues the Mu'tazili position on human action is only terminologically different, but then shows its logical collapse.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why al-Nasafi argues that the dispute with the Mu'tazila over human action is largely terminological, but also identifies a deeper theological error that invalidates their entire position.

Core Idea
The Mu'tazili and Ash'ari positions on human power diverge only in name, but deeper disagreement lies in God's sovereignty.

Al-Nasafi tackles the longstanding debate over human action. He first notes that both sides agree on the limited scope of human power; the only difference is what they call it. However, he then reveals a critical point where the Mu'tazila depart from reason, aligning with those who deny God's omnipotence. Let's follow his reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the terminological agreement
Read the passage and note that al-Nasafi concedes the Mu'tazila could only attach the servant's power to the same limited sphere he does. The difference is only in terminology: they call it 'creation' (khalq) and 'invention' (ikhtira'), while he calls it 'acquisition' (kasb).
This is the point where the dispute appears merely semantic.
2
Identify the deeper disagreement
Beyond the servant's sphere, al-Nasafi affirms that everything is under God's power. The Mu'tazila, however, deny that anyone has power over that realm. This, he argues, aligns them with the Dahriyya (materialists) and Mu'attila (those who nullify God's attributes).
Here the difference becomes substantive, not just terminological.
3
See the irrational conclusion
The Mu'tazila restrict power's attachment only to existence, but existence is not a separate meaning from essence. Thus power would have no object, which is impossible. This invalidates all their principles.
This is the core of al-Nasafi's refutation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وراء الشيئية، فينبغي أن لا تتعلق قدرة العبد به فلم يمكنهم تعليق قدرة العبد إلا بقدر ما قلنا، فإذا قلنا بمثل ما قالوا ولم يبق بيننا خلاف إلا في العبارة فإنهم سموا ذلك خلقًا واختراعا، ونحن سميناه كسبًا إلا أن ما وراء ذلك عندنا متعلق بقدرة الله تعالى وعندهم لا يقدره أحد فاستوينا في جنبة العبد ولم يبق لهم علينا إشكال، وفيما وراء ذلك التحقوا بالدهرية والمعطلة، ونحن ثبنا على القول بثبوت الصانع بل هم أتوا بما هو غير المعقول وأبطلوا القول بتعلق القدرة بالمقدور، فإنهم أحالوا تعلقها إلا بالوجود والوجود راجع إلى الذات إذ هو ليس بمعنى وراء الذات ولا تعلق لها بالذات والشيئية فإن تعلقها بما لا تعلق لها به وهو محال وهذيان وبطل بهذا الفصل جميع قواعد المعتزلة وأضمحلت شبهاتهم، ونحن نحمد الله في غاية الوضوح.
Beyond thingness, it is necessary that the power of the servant should not attach to it, so they could not attach the servant's power except to the extent we said. So when we say similarly to what they said and no disagreement remains between us except in expression—for they named that creation and invention, while we named it acquisition—except that what is beyond that is with us dependent on the power of God Most High, while with them no one has power over it. So we are equal regarding the side of the servant, and no problem remains for us from them. But regarding what is beyond that, they joined the materialists (Dahriyya) and the nullifiers (Mu'attila). We have remained firm upon the statement affirming the Maker. Rather, they came with what is irrational and invalidated the statement of the attachment of power to the object of power, for they considered its attachment impossible except to existence, and existence reverts to essence since it is not a meaning beyond essence, and power has no attachment to essence and thingness. For its attachment to that to which it has no attachment is impossible and nonsense. By this chapter all the principles of the Mu'tazila are invalidated and their specious arguments are demolished. And we praise God to the utmost clarity.
Source pages p. 920

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the Mu'tazili position on human action. The author begins by noting that the Mu'tazila could only attach the servant's power to a limited sphere, just as he himself does. Thus the dispute reduces to a mere terminological difference: they call it 'creation' (khalaq) and 'invention' (ikhtira'), while he calls it 'acquisition' (kasb). However, the author then points out a critical divergence: beyond that limited sphere, he maintains that all things are under God's power, whereas the Mu'tazila deny that anyone has power over them. This, he argues, aligns them with the Dahriyya (materialists who deny God) and the Mu'attila (those who nullify God's attributes). He then accuses them of irrationality: they restrict power's attachment only to 'existence', but existence is not a separate meaning from essence; thus power would have no object. This, he concludes, demolishes all Mu'tazili principles.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Ash'ari-Mu'tazili dispute on human action is purely semantic.
Correction: Al-Nasafi shows that while on the surface the difference is terminological, the Mu'tazila's position on what lies beyond the servant's power leads to a denial of God's omnipotence and irrational conclusions, making the disagreement substantive.

Checkpoint
According to the author, where do the Mu'tazila go beyond mere terminological difference?
A) They affirm that nothing is impossible for God.
B) They deny that anyone has power over what is beyond the servant's limited sphere.
C) They agree with the author on everything.
D) They reject the term 'acquisition'.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazili and Ash'ari agree on the scope of human power but differ terminologically; however the Mu'tazili denial of God's power beyond that sphere is irrational and aligns them with atheists.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

How might that reflect a casual attitude toward divine attributes? This week, when you encounter a doctrinal difference, will you seek to understand the underlying principles before labeling it merely verbal?
?
The Mu'tazila are accused of implicitly aligning with atheists by limiting God's power. Is there any area of your life where you functionally deny God's power—perhaps in your career, relationships, or fears—by acting as if outcomes are entirely in your hands? What would it mean for you to truly affirm His absolute power over everything beyond your tiny sphere?
?
If the slightest logical flaw can demolish an entire theological framework, how rigorously do you examine your own beliefs? Are you willing to follow the argument wherever it leads, even if it challenges comfortable assumptions? Set aside time this week to identify one belief you hold about divine decree and test it against the Quran and scholarly consensus, not just habit.

Symmetrical Refutation of Three Mu'tazili Objections
The author answers three Mu'tazili objections by turning their own premises against them.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author uses the Mu'tazili's own denial of human effective action to turn their objections about punishment back against them.

Core Idea
The Mu'tazili's premises are a double-edged sword that cuts their own argument.

This passage showcases a powerful dialectical technique. The Mu'tazili object that if God creates actions, punishment is unjust. The author responds by showing that the Mu'tazili's own view—that humans create nothing—makes punishment equally inexplicable. By flipping the question, the author forces them into a symmetrical dilemma.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and note the three objections
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Identify the three distinct objections raised by the Mu'tazila and the author's responses.
Notice that each objection and response follows the same pattern.
2
Identify the common structure
For each objection, the author takes the Mu'tazili's own premise (that the human does nothing) and applies it to the same situation, creating a symmetrical problem: 'If you say God does X, then what did you do? But if you say you do nothing, then on what basis are you punished?'
This is a classic a fortiori or tu quoque argument.
3
Examine the underlying premise
The Mu'tazili's claim that existence is identical with essence (not a separate attribute) and that human power does not attach to 'thingness' or 'essenceness' is key. The author uses this to argue that if the human does not create the essence, then punishment cannot be for the action's existence.
This metaphysical premise is crucial for the symmetry.
4
Reflect on the rhetorical strength
Consider why this symmetrical refutation is effective: it shows that the Mu'tazili cannot object without contradicting themselves. The author does not need to provide a positive alternative—just demonstrates the inconsistency.
This method is often used in kalam to neutralize objections.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبهذا يجابون عن قولهم: إن الله تعالى إذا فعل الحركة فماذا فعلت أنا ؟ فيقال لهم وإذا لم تفعل أنت الشيء ولا العين والوجود هو عين الشيء إذ ليس بمعنى وراءه فماذا فعلت إذا لم يفعل الله الجوهر ولا العرض ولا الشيء ولا العين ولا السواد ولا البياض ولا الرائحة ولا الطعم فماذا فعل؟ ووجودها إذا لم يكن معنى وراءها أو هو عينها وذاتها ولم يفعل عينها ولا ذاتها فماذا فعل؟ فما أجابوا من شيء فهو لهم جواب. وكذا الجواب عن قولهم : كيف يجوز أن يقال يخلق الله تعالى في يد إنسان سرقه ثم يأمر بقطعها ؟ وكذا هذا في الزنا وكذا يخلق فعلاً ثم يعاقب عليه؟ قيل: وإذا لم يفعل السارق شيئًا ولا الزاني ولا الكافر ، وكان الزنا والسرقة والكفر في العدم أشياء وأعراضاً وأعيانا ووجودها أعيانها أو ليس معنى وراءها ولم تتعلق قدرة العبد بالشيئية ولا العرضية ولا العينية، ولم تتعلق بمعنى وراءها فماذا فعله، وعلى أي فعل يقطع ويحد ويعاقب ؟ فما أجابوا فهو جواب لهم. وكذا الجواب عن قولهم: إذا لم يكن وجود إلا للخالق ولمخلوقه أفيعاقب على وجود الخالق أم على وجود المخلوق؟ قيل: وإذا لم يوجد إلا شيء لم يفعله أحد أفيعاقب على الشيء؟ فما أجابوا فهو لهم جواب.
And by this they are answered regarding their statement: 'If God Most High creates the motion, what did I do?' So it is said to them: If you (the servant) do not do the thing nor the essence—and existence is the very essence of the thing since it is not a meaning beyond it—then what did you do when God does not create the substance, nor the accident, nor the thing, nor the essence, nor blackness, nor whiteness, nor smell, nor taste? So what did you do? And their existence, if it is not a meaning beyond them or it is their very essence and entity, and He did not create their essence nor their entity, so what did He do? Whatever they answer is an answer for them. Likewise the answer to their statement: 'How can it be said that God Most High creates theft in the hand of a person and then commands to cut it off?' Likewise this regarding fornication, and likewise He creates an action and then punishes for it? It is said: And if the thief does nothing, nor the fornicator, nor the disbeliever, and fornication, theft, and disbelief in non-existence are things, accidents, and essences, and their existence is their essences or is not a meaning beyond them, and the power of the servant does not attach to thingness nor accidentness nor essence-ness, and does not attach to a meaning beyond them, then what did he do, and for which action is he amputated, punished with prescribed punishment, and punished? Whatever they answer is an answer for them. Likewise the answer to their statement: 'If there is no existence except for the Creator and for His creature, is he punished for the existence of the Creator or for the existence of the creature?' It is said: And if nothing exists except a thing that no one did, is he punished for the thing? Whatever they answer is an answer for them.
Source pages p. 920

Commentary
This passage is a series of three objections-and-answers, each refuted by turning the Mu'tazili's own premises against them. The first objection: if God creates movement, what did I do? Answer: If, according to you, the human does not do the thing nor its essence (since existence is not separate from essence), and God does not create substance, accident, etc., then what did the human do? The question is symmetrical. The second objection: how can God create theft in a hand and then command to cut it off? Answer: If the thief does nothing, and theft in non-existence is a thing with attributes like 'thingness', and human power does not attach to those attributes, then on what basis is he punished? Again, the Mu'tazili must answer their own question. The third objection: if existence is only from God, does punishment attach to God's existence or the creature's? Answer: If nothing exists except a thing that no one did, is punishment for the thing? The pattern is consistent: the Mu'tazili denial of any effective human action leaves them unable to explain the basis of punishment.

Correction: The author shows that the same objections apply to the Mu'tazili position, revealing it to be equally (if not more) problematic.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazili's own logic forces them into a contradiction when applied to the problem of punishment.

If your actions do not exist independently of God's power, then every deed—good or bad—is an occasion to recognize your absolute dependence on Him. In what area of your life are you most tempted to feel self-sufficient? When you succeed, do you inwardly attribute the success to your own effort? How can you train yourself, starting this week, to pause and say 'Allah created this moment' before you react?

The Real Proof: Kasb and Moral Accountability
The author states the above answers are gratuitous, and the real proof is that the servant has a kasb.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the Maturidi-Ash'ari proof for human action (kasb) does not rely on dialectical arguments but on the established fact that humans have an action that grounds moral accountability, even though they are not creators.

Core Idea
The real proof is not argument but the fact of human moral accountability.

In this concluding passage, the author steps back from the preceding dialectical exchanges to state explicitly what his real proof is. He explains that all the earlier responses were voluntary—helpful, but not the foundation. The true proof (dalaala) is simply that the servant possesses an action (fi'l) even without being a creator, and that this action is sufficient to justify punishment. This is the kernel of the kasb theory.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the literal translation. Notice the sentence is incomplete; the key point is that the author distinguishes between voluntary answers and the real proof.
The passage is a conclusion; it summarizes the author's position.
2
Identify the contrast
The author contrasts two things: the preceding answers (تبرع) and the established proof (الدلالة). Identify what each refers to.
تبرع means voluntary, not essential. الدلالة is the actual evidence.
3
Understand the concept of kasb
The real proof is that the servant has an action (له فعل) even though he is not a creator. This action is kasb. Even if we can't understand its modality, it grounds punishment.
This is the core of Maturidi/Ash'ari occasionalism.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وهذا كله تبرع منا فإنا إذا أقمنا الدلالة على أن العبد وإن لم يكن مخترعا خالقا فله فعل وإن كان لا يقع كيفية ذلك في أوهامنا، فنقول: بأنه يعاقب
And all this is a voluntary act from us, for we have established the proof that the servant, even if he is not an inventor or creator, has an action, even if the modality of that does not occur in our imaginations. So we say: that he is punished...
Source pages p. 921

Commentary
This passage is a concluding remark by the author. He explains that the preceding dialectical answers are voluntary (tabarra')—meaning they are not the primary proof. The real established proof (dalaala) is that the servant has an action (fi'l) even though he is not an inventor (mukhtari') or creator (khaliq). This action is the 'kasb' (acquisition). Even if we cannot fully comprehend its modality (kayfiyya), it is sufficient to justify punishment. The text breaks off with 'that he is punished' (annahu yu'aqqab), suggesting the continuation would elaborate. The key point is that the kasb theory provides a basis for moral accountability without requiring the servant to be a creator.

Answering the Objection: What Did the Human Do?
Answers the objection: if God creates movement, what does the human do?

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Maturidi response refutes the objection that if God creates movement, human action is meaningless.

Core Idea
Human acts are real even though God creates the essence of all things.

This passage from Imam al-Nasafi addresses a classic qadar dilemma: if God creates everything, what is left for human action? The author turns the tables on the objector by showing that their reasoning leads to absurdity. We will see how the argument works by analyzing the text closely.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic text once without analyzing, then read the literal translation. Let the argument's structure emerge naturally.
Notice the rhetorical device: the author answers a question with another question.
2
Identify the objection and the counter-argument
The objector asks: if God creates motion, what did I do? The author responds by asking: if you did not create the thing itself (since existence is identical to the thing), what did you do? Then extends to show that if God did not create any attribute, nothing was created.
The key move is equating existence with the essence (عین) of a thing.
3
Understand the key concept and conclusion
Grasp that existence (وجود) is not a separate attribute but the very reality of a thing. Hence, if the human does not create the thing's essence, he cannot claim to have created anything. The objector's own logic is turned against them.
This is a powerful refutation that preserves the reality of human action without denying divine creation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبهذا يجابون عن قولهم: إن الله تعالى إذا فعل الحركة فماذا فعلت أنا ؟ فيقال لهم وإذا لم تفعل أنت الشيء ولا العين ( والوجود هو عين الشيء إذ ليس بمعنى وراءه فماذا فعلت إذا لم يفعل الله الجوهر ولا العرض ولا الشيء ولا العين) ولا السواد ولا البياض ولا الرائحة ولا الطعم فماذا فعل؟ ووجودها إذا لم يكن معنى وراءها أو هو عينها وذاتها ولم يفعل عينها ولا ذاتها فماذا فعل؟ فما أجابوا من شيء فهو لهم جواب.
And by this they are answered concerning their statement: 'If God, exalted is He, creates motion, then what did I do?' So it is said to them: 'And if you did not do the thing nor the entity (and existence is the entity of the thing since it is not a meaning behind it), so what did you do if God did not create the substance, nor the accident, nor the thing, nor the entity, nor blackness, nor whiteness, nor smell, nor taste? So what did He create? And their existence, if there is no meaning behind them or it is their entity and essence, and He did not create their entity nor their essence, what did He create? Whatever they answer, it is a reply against them.'
Source pages p. 921

Commentary
This passage presents an argument against those who ask: if God creates motion, what does the human do? The author turns the question back: if the human does not create the thing itself (since existence is the same as the thing, not a separate meaning), then what did the human do? And if God did not create the substance, accident, etc., what did He create? The point is that existence is not a separate attribute but the very essence of the thing. So if the human does not create the essence, they cannot claim to have done anything. The answer is that the human's act is real, and the objection is based on a misunderstanding.

Key Terms
فعل
عين
وجود
جوهر
عرض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Human actions have no reality if God creates all things.
Correction: The passage argues that human action is real and not negated by divine creation, because the human does not create the essence of things—that belongs to God—but the human's act is still genuine.

Checkpoint

A) The human created them
B) Nothing was created at all
C) The human did something else
D) God created them later

Key Takeaway
The objection that divine creation renders human action meaningless is refuted by showing that existence is identical to essence, so the human's act cannot be creation but is nevertheless real.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you truly believe that God creates every atom of your existence, do you still feel fully accountable for your sins? Or is there a part of you that secretly blames God for your failures? This lesson forces you to confront that: you did not create your actions, but they are still yours. Will you own them today, without excuse?
?
When you perform a good deed, do you ever feel proud as if you created it yourself? And when you sin, do you feel a sense of distance from it, as if God created it and you were just a vessel? How can you humbly accept that your acts are real, yet completely dependent on God? What will you change in your attitude toward your deeds after this lesson?
?
This lesson argues that whatever the objector answers, it becomes a reply against them. In your own life, when you make excuses (like 'God made me do it' or 'I couldn't help it'), what does that reveal about your heart? Are you willing to tear down that excuse and admit that your actions are truly yours, even though God creates them? What practical step can you take today to stop blaming God for your choices?

Answering the Objection on Divine Creation of Acts and Punishment
Refutation of an objection against divine predestination and punishment

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Maturidi response resolves the apparent contradiction between God creating all acts and punishing humans for those acts, through the distinction between divine creation and human acquisition.

Core Idea
God's creation and human acquisition are distinct—punishment is for human kasb, not God's creation.

This lesson tackles a classic objection raised against the Ash'ari/Maturidi position on qadar: if God creates all actions, including theft and disbelief, how can He justly punish the human? The author presents the objection and then turns it on its head, showing that the questioner's own assumptions lead to absurdities. By examining this debate, we grasp the crucial distinction between divine creation and human acquisition.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection as presented
Carefully read the first part of the Arabic passage, where the objector asks how God can create theft in a hand and then command its amputation. This sets up the problem.
The objection assumes that if God creates the act, the human is not responsible.
2
Understand the author's inversion
The author responds by inverting the objection: if the human does not really do anything (since all existence is from God), then what act is being punished? This exposes a flaw in the objector's reasoning.
The author is using a reductio ad absurdum argument.
3
Identify the fallacy in the objector's logic
Notice that the objector assumes existence is the only reality, ignoring the human's acquisition (kasb). The author shows that if the human's act is not real, there is no basis for punishment, which the objector would also reject.
The fallacy is treating God's creation as identical to the human's act.
4
Reflect on the underlying kasb doctrine
The Maturidi doctrine of kasb holds that while God creates the act, the human 'acquires' it, making the human morally responsible. This distinction answers the objection.
The kasb concept is central to resolving theodicy issues in qadar.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكذا الجواب عن قولهم : كيف يجوز أن يقال يخلق الله تعالى في يد إنسان سرقه ثم يأمر بقطعها ؟ وكذا هذا في الزنا وكذا يخلق فعلاً ثم يعاقب عليه؟ قيل: وإذا لم يفعل السارق شيئًا ولا الزاني ولا الكافر ، وكان الزنا والسرقة والكفر في العدم أشياء وأعراضاً وأعيانا ووجودها أعيانها أو ليس معنى وراءها ولم تتعلق قدرة العبد بالشيئية ولا العرضية ولا العينية، ولم تتعلق بمعنى وراءها فماذا فعله، وعلى أي فعل يقطع ويحد ويعاقب ؟ فما أجابوا فهو جواب لهم.
And likewise is the answer to their statement: 'How is it allowed to say that God, exalted is He, creates in the hand of a man theft then commands its amputation?' And similarly regarding fornication, and similarly He creates an act then punishes for it? It is said: 'And if the thief did not do anything, nor the fornicator, nor the disbeliever, and fornication, theft, and disbelief in non-existence were things, accidents, and entities, and their existence is their entities or not a meaning behind them, and the slave's power does not pertain to thingness, accidentness, or entityness, and does not pertain to a meaning behind them, so what did he do, and for which act is he amputated, flogged, and punished? Whatever they answer, it is a reply against them.'
Source pages p. 921

Commentary
This passage deals with a common theological objection: if God creates the act of theft in a person's hand, how can God then command that the hand be cut off? Similarly for fornication and disbelief, God would be punishing for His own creation. The author refutes by inverting: if the thief does not really do anything (since all existence is from God), then what act is being punished? The point is that the human's act is real, and the objection assumes that existence is the only reality, ignoring the human's acquisition (kasb). The author argues that the questioner's own assumptions lead to absurdities.

Key Terms
خلق
سرقة
زنا
كفر
قدرة العبد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If God creates the act, then punishing the human is unjust and God is cruel.
Correction: God creates the act but the human acquires it (kasb), making the human responsible. The objection fails to distinguish between creation and acquisition.

Checkpoint
What does the author say the thief did not do?
A) He did not steal
B) He did not do anything at all
C) He did not exist
D) He did not choose

Key Takeaway
The apparent injustice of God punishing for created acts disappears when we distinguish divine creation from human acquisition (kasb).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever excused your own sin by thinking, 'God wrote it for me, so it's not my fault'? How does this passage force you to own your actions instead of blaming God? What one sin will you stop making excuses for starting today?
?
The objector questions God's justice, but the author turns the logic around. When you sin, do you question God's justice, or do you question your own choices? How will you change your inner dialogue to take full responsibility before Him?
?
If punishment has no meaning without your involvement, then your salvation also requires your kasb. Are you actively acquiring good deeds, or passively waiting for God's grace? What is one concrete act of obedience you will acquire today, knowing that you are responsible for it?

Answering the Objection: Punishment for Whose Existence?
Answer to the objection about punishing for existence created by God

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Maturidi response reframes the objection about punishment for existence created by God, and why the author considers these answers a voluntary favor (tabarru').

Core Idea
The objection about punishing for God's existence collapses when turned back on itself.

The opponents ask: if only God and His creation exist, is punishment for God's existence or the creation's? Sanusi in the Maturidi tradition answers by reversing the question, showing its absurdity. But then he adds that such answers are voluntary—the real proof is that the human has an act (fi'l) for which he is responsible. This lesson unpacks both the argument and the author's methodological note.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to grasp the flow. Note the objection first, then the response.
Focus on the phrase 'وهذا كله تبرع منا'
2
Understand the reversal
The author turns the question: if a thing exists without any creator, is punishment for that thing? This shows the original question is meaningless.
This is a common rhetorical technique in kalam.
3
Grasp the key distinction
The real answer is that humans have an act (fi'l) even if not creators. The objection attempts to make that act illusory, but that is invalid.
The author calls these answers 'voluntary' because the real proof already stands.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكذا الجواب عن قولهم: إذا لم يكن وجود إلا للخالق ولمخلوقه أفيعاقب على وجود الخالق أم على وجود المخلوق؟ قيل: وإذا لم يوجد إلا شيء لم يفعله أحد أفيعاقب على الشيء؟ فما أجابوا فهو لهم جواب، وهذا كله تبرع منا فإنا إذا أقمنا الدلالة على أن العبد وإن لم يكن مخترعا خالقا فله فعل وإن كان لا يقع كيفية ذلك في أوهامنا، فنقول: بأنه يعاقب على فعله، وكل هذه الأسئلة طلب لجعل فعلنا موهوماً وهو فاسد، فلا ينبغي أن نصغي إليها غير أنا بينا هذه الأجوبة على طريق التبرع ليتبين بذلك وهاء كلامهم وأنهم لا يلزمون إلا ما يلزمهم مثله ولا يعيبون خصومهم إلا بما هم معيبون به بل هم المعيبون دون خصومهم على ما قررنا والله الموفق.
And likewise the answer to their statement: 'If existence is only from the Creator and His creation, is he punished for the existence of the Creator or for the existence of the creation?' It is said: 'And if nothing exists except a thing that no one did, is he punished for the thing?' Whatever they answer, it is a reply against them. And all of this is a voluntary favor from us, for if we establish the proof that the slave, though not an inventor or creator, has an act even if the modality of that does not occur in our imaginations, then we say: he is punished for his act. And all these questions are a demand to make our act illusory, and it is corrupt. So we should not listen to them, except that we have presented these answers by way of voluntary favor to make clear thereby the weakness of their speech, and that they only charge others with what is equally chargeable against them, and they do not fault their opponents except with what they themselves are faulted with, rather they are the faulted ones, not their opponents, as we have established. And God is the grantor of success.
Source pages p. 921

Commentary
This passage contains the third objection: if only God's existence and the creation's existence exist, then when a human is punished, is it for God's existence or the creation's existence? The author turns the question: if there is only a thing that no one created, then punishment is for that thing—making the question absurd. Then the author explains that these answers are given voluntarily, because the real proof is that the human has an act (fi'l) even if not a creator. The questions are invalid because they try to make our act something illusory. The purpose is to show the weakness of the opponents' arguments: they accuse others of faults that apply to themselves.

Key Terms
وجود
فعل العبد
اختراع
تبرع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that if God creates all existence, human responsibility becomes meaningless, so punishment cannot be just.
Correction: The author insists that humans have a real act (fi'l) that is not mere existence, and punishment is for that act, not for existence per se.

Checkpoint
Why does the author call these answers 'voluntary' (تبرع)?
A) Because the opponent asked politely
B) Because the real proof is already established
C) Because the author is being generous
D) Because the questions are easy

Key Takeaway
The author shows that the objection self-destructs when turned around, and the real ground for human responsibility is the human's own act (fi'l), not merely existence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you reflect on your own sins, do you ever feel a sense of injustice because God created your existence and your inclinations? How does the author's response in this passage directly confront that feeling? Will you now take full responsibility for your actions, knowing that your act is truly yours even if God created existence?
?
The author says the opponents' questions are 'a demand to make our act illusory.' In your daily life, when have you tried to make your own wrong actions seem illusory or shift blame to circumstances or others? How can this passage change that habit? What will you do the next time you catch yourself doing that?
?
The author gives an answer as a voluntary favor, but insists the real proof is that you have an act. If you truly believed that your act is your own and that you are accountable for it, how would it change the way you think about repentance? What concrete step will you take today to own your actions and turn to God sincerely?

Refuting the Division of Human Acts into Parts
Refutes the division of act into entirely from God, human, or both

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Maturidi scholar rejects the tripartite division of human actions into entirely from God, entirely from human, or partly from both, based on the indivisible nature of accidents.

Core Idea
Acts are accidents; accidents cannot be divided into parts.

The objectors attempt to force a dilemma: either the act is entirely from God, entirely from human, or shared. The author points out a logical flaw—this division assumes acts can be broken into parts like bodies. But acts are accidents, and accidents are indivisible. Thus the entire objection is built on a mistaken premise.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection presented
Identify the three possibilities the objector claims are exhaustive: act entirely from God, entirely from human, or part from each.
This is a common Mu'tazili argument.
2
Understand the refutation regarding accidents
The author explains that division into parts is valid for bodies but not for accidents. Acts are accidents, so they cannot be partitioned.
Accidents lack composition and continuity.
3
Examine the follow-up argument
The author then argues about blame: blame attaches to the act itself, not to the thingness or essence, and similarly, God is blamed for His invention?
This part is cut off but shows further reasoning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبهذا يجابون عن قولهم: إن فعل العبد لا يخلو إما أن يكون كله من الله تعالى فيذم هو عليه، وإما أن يكون كله من العبد وهو ما قلنا، وإما أن يكون بعضه من الله تعالى وبعضه من العبد فيشتركان في الذم . فيقال لهم: لو عقلتم لما اشتغلتم بهذا التقسيم إذ هذا مما يصح في الأجسام لما متبعضة متجزئة دون الأعراض التي يستحيل عليها التبعض والتجزؤ لانعدام تألقها وتركبها ، وما لا بعض له لا كل له إذ هما من الأسماء الإضافية والتقسيم (١) إلى الكل والبعض، فما يستحيل عليه البعض والكل جهل. ثم نقول لهم هل مفعول الله تعالى وهو فعله فيذم على فعله كما هو عندكم شيء وذات وعين وموجود (٢) ويذم على وجوده دون شيئيته وذاتيته وعينيته، على أنا إذا أثبتنا بالدليل أن له فعلاً و لا (٣) اختراع (٤) له فيذم هو على فعله ولا يذم الله = تعالى على اختراعه لما نبين بعد هذا - إن شاء الله تعالى كما يذم عندكم على وجود فعله دون شيئيته على أن هذا يلزم أبا الهذيل وأبا موسى(٥) المردار حيث جعلا حركة الحجر فعلاً للمحركين، ولو كان أحدهما مأمورًا بالتحريك والآخر منهيًا عنه فتكون الحركة في ذاتها شيئًا واحدًا وهي طاعة ومعصية يحمد المطيع
And by this they are answered concerning their statement: 'The slave's act is either entirely from God, so He is blamed for it; or entirely from the slave, which is what we said; or partly from God and partly from the slave, so they share in blame.' So it is said to them: 'If you understood, you would not occupy yourselves with this division, for this is valid for bodies that are divisible and partitionable, not for accidents which are impossible to be partitioned or divided due to the absence of their continuity and composition. And whatever has no part has no whole, for they are among relative names, and division into whole and part is applied. So what is impossible for it to have a part or whole is ignorance. Then we say to them: Is the effect of God, which is His act, blamed for His act as it is according to you a thing, essence, entity, and existent, and He is blamed for its existence without its thingness, essenceness, and entityness? And if we establish by proof that He has an act and no invention, then he is blamed for his act and God is not blamed for His invention, as we will explain after this—God willing—just as according to you He is blamed for the existence of His act without its thingness. And this applies to Abu al-Hudhayl and Abu Musa al-Murdar where they made the motion of a stone an act of the movers, and if one of them was commanded to move and the other prohibited, then the motion in its essence is one thing, and it is obedience and disobedience; the obedient is praised...'
Source pages p. 922

Commentary
This passage refutes an objection based on a tripartite division of action. The objector says: either the act is entirely from God (so He is blamed), entirely from the human (which is what we say), or partly from both (so they are both blamed). The author responds by noting that this division applies to bodies that can be divided into parts, but accidents (including actions) cannot be divided because they lack continuity (ta'alluq) and composition. If something has no part, it cannot have a whole; these are relational concepts. Thus the division is inapplicable. Then the author further argues: according to the objectors, God's act (which is His effect) is blamed as a thing, essence, etc., but only blamed for its existence, not its thingness. Similarly, if the human has an act (not creation), he is blamed for his act, not God for His creation. The author then notes that this objection also applies to Abu al-Hudhayl and Abu Musa al-Murdar, who considered the motion of a stone as the act of the movers, leading to the paradox that one motion could be both obedience and disobedience. The passage ends mid-sentence at the page break.

Key Terms
فعل العبد
تقسيم
أعراض
تبعض
أبو الهذيل
أبو موسى المردار

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Actions can be divided into parts like bodies.
Correction: Actions are accidents, and accidents are indivisible because they lack composition and continuity.

Checkpoint
Why does the author reject the division of the act into parts?
A) Because the act is from God only
B) Because acts are accidents and accidents cannot be divided
C) Because the human has no act
D) Because the division is illogical

Key Takeaway
The objection that an act must be wholly from God, wholly from human, or shared fails because it treats acts as divisible bodies, but acts are accidents and thus indivisible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you sin, do you ever find yourself mentally dividing the act into 'partly my fault, partly God's decree' to soften your guilt? How does the indivisibility of acts force you to own your sin fully?
?
If blame attaches to the existence of an act rather than its essence, what does that mean for how you should view your own good deeds? Are you more concerned with the 'essence' of the deed (its label) or the fact that you actually did it?
?
This passage shows that dividing an act into parts leads to logical absurdities. In your daily choices, do you ever try to compartmentalize your intentions—'this part is for God, this part for my own benefit'? How might that be a form of the very division the author refutes?

Refuting Abu al-Hudhayl's Compulsion in Paradise
Nasafi presents a dilemma against Abu al-Hudhayl's compulsion in Paradise.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Nasafi refutes Abu al-Hudhayl's deterministic view of actions in Paradise using a logical dilemma.

Core Idea
Abu al-Hudhayl's compulsion in Paradise leads to attributing human acts to God.

Abu al-Hudhayl, a Mu'tazilite leader, held that the people of Paradise are compelled in all their actions. Nasafi here exposes the absurd consequence of that view: if all actions are God's, then God becomes the agent of eating, drinking, etc. This short but powerful argument uses a dilemma to force Abu al-Hudhayl into either blasphemy or contradiction.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Let the argument unfold as Nasafi presents it.
2
Identify the doctrine
Find the statement of Abu al-Hudhayl's view: people in paradise are compelled in their actions and all actions are God's doing without any human choice.
3
Notice the list of actions
Nasafi lists specific actions (eating, drinking, walking, etc.). Note that these actions are normally attributed to humans, but Abu al-Hudhayl attributes them entirely to God.
4
Understand the dilemma
Nasafi presents a choice for Abu al-Hudhayl: either accept the blasphemous consequence (God does all these acts) or reject the original claim of compulsion. Either way his position fails.
The dilemma is a powerful refutation tool in kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم من مذهب رئيسكم أبي الهذيل أن أهل الجنة مجبورون على أفعالهم وما يوجد منهم من الأكل والشرب والجماع والمشي والقعود والقبض والبسط كله فعل الله تعالى بلا اختيار للعبد حتى قيل: إن أبا الهذيل جهمي الآخرة فيكون على قود كلامه هذا الآكل الشارب المجامع الماشي القاعد القابض الباسط هو الله تعالى الله عما يقولون علوا كبيرًا - فكان هو بين أمرين إما أن يلتزم ذلك كله فيكفر، وإما أن يمتنع فيبطل حجاجه.
Then from the doctrine of your leader Abu al-Hudhayl is that the people of Paradise are compelled in their actions, and what occurs from them of eating, drinking, sexual intercourse, walking, sitting, grasping, and spreading—all of it is the action of God without any choice for the servant, until it was said: 'Abu al-Hudhayl is a Jahmī of the Hereafter.' Thus according to his statement, the eater, the drinker, the fornicator, the walker, the sitter, the grasper, the spreader would be God—exalted is God above what they say, with great exaltation. So he is between two matters: either he commits to all of that and thus becomes a disbeliever, or he refrains and his argument becomes invalid.
Source pages p. 923

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of Abu al-Hudhayl's doctrine that the inhabitants of Paradise are compelled (mujbarūn) in their actions, and that all their actions—such as eating, drinking, intercourse, walking, etc.—are entirely God's actions without any human choice. Nasafi points out the absurd consequence: if every action is directly God's act, then God himself would be the one eating, drinking, etc. This is clearly blasphemous. He then presents a dilemma for Abu al-Hudhayl: either he accepts this blasphemous attribution and becomes a disbeliever, or he denies it and his original argument falls apart. The label 'Jahmī of the Hereafter' refers to Abu al-Hudhayl's extreme determinism regarding paradise.

Key Terms
أبي الهذيل
أهل الجنة
مجبورون
فعل الله تعالى
جهمي الآخرة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The belief that humans in Paradise are compelled in all their actions and that God is the true agent of those actions.
Correction: Nasafi shows this leads to attributing human acts (eating, drinking, etc.) to God, which is blasphemous. Humans in paradise act by God's creation but with their own choice.

Checkpoint
What dilemma does Nasafi present to Abu al-Hudhayl?
A) Either paradise is temporary or eternal
B) Either he accepts that God does all human acts in paradise and thus becomes a disbeliever, or he denies it and his argument collapses
C) Either humans have free will or they don't
D) Either actions are created by God or by humans

Key Takeaway
Abu al-Hudhayl's view of compulsion in Paradise leads to the blasphemous conclusion that God performs human actions, and the dilemma forces him into contradiction.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If someone claimed that your every action, even your prayers in Paradise, were not truly yours but God's, how would that change how you view your own worship and gratitude?
?
Are there areas of your life where you secretly act as if you are compelled—blaming God for your shortcomings while taking credit for your successes? What would change if you fully owned your choices?
?
The dilemma Nasafi presents forces Abu al-Hudhayl into an impossible position. Is there a belief you hold that, if followed to its logical conclusion, would lead to something you know is false? Will you examine it today?

Refuting the Claim that the Servant's Act Makes Him a Creator
Nasafi refutes the objection that the servant's act makes him a creator.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Nasafi argues that the name of an act refers to the one in whom the act's meaning resides, and how this principle refutes the objection that the servant is a creator.

Core Idea
Doing an act does not make one a creator unless done alone with exclusive power.

In this passage, Imam Nasafi addresses a key objection from those who argue that if the servant performs a created act, then the servant must be a creator. He responds by establishing a foundational principle: the name derived from an action refers to the one in whom the action's meaning inheres, not merely to the one who brings it into existence. This distinction allows him to explain that the servant is an 'acquirer' (muktasib) rather than a creator, and he further exposes the flaw in the opponents' reasoning through a clever reductio argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Focus on Nasafi's opening principle about names and meanings. Then follow his response to the objection and the reductio.
2
Identify the principle about names
What does Nasafi say determines who is named after an act? How does this apply to the servant's act?
The name refers to the one in whom the meaning resides, not to the one who brings the meaning into existence.
3
Follow the response to the creator objection
Notice the distinction between doing alone and not alone, and the terms creator vs. acquirer.
If done alone, it's a creator; if not alone, it's an acquirer.
4
Understand the reductio argument
See how Nasafi turns the opponents' logic against them using the concept of 'thing-maker'.
This shows the inconsistency in their position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والحاصل : أن الاسم المقدر عن المعنى يكون راجعا إلى من قام به المعنى كان ذلك كسبًا له كالحركة الاختيارية أو لم يكن كالحركة الضرورية لا إلى موجد المعنى فكان هذا السؤال باطلاً. وبهذا يجابون عن قولهم : إن العبد عندكم يفعل المخلوق ومن فعل المخلوق فهو خالق فيقال لهم من فعل المخلوق فهو فاعل، فبعد ذلك إن كان فعل على الانفراد فهو خالق، وإن كان فعل لا على الانفراد فهو مكتسب، وهذا على عبارة القائلين إن التكوين هو المكون، وعبارتنا أن من فعله المخلوق فهو فاعل ومن فعل المخلوق فهو خالق، وجميع ما يوردون من نحو هذه الأسئلة يجاب على هذا الطريق، ثم يقال لهم: على قياس قولكم ينبغي أن يقال: إن من فعل الشيء فهو مشيء فإن أقروا به أدخلوا الشيئية تحت الفعل ورجعوا عن قولهم: إن المعدوم شيء، وإن أنكروا بطل سؤالهم.
The summary: that the name designated by the meaning refers to the one in whom the meaning resides, whether that is an acquisition for him like voluntary movement or not like involuntary movement, not to the one who brings the meaning into existence. So this question is invalid. And by this they answer their statement: 'Verily the servant according to you does the created thing, and whoever does a created thing is a creator.' So it is said to them: whoever does a created thing is a doer. Then after that, if he did it alone, he is a creator; if he did it not alone, he is an acquirer. This is according to the phrasing of those who say that coming-into-being is the same as the thing coming-into-being, and according to our phrasing: whoever does the created thing is a doer, and whoever does the created thing is a creator. And all the questions they bring of this kind are answered in this way. Then it is said to them: on the analogy of your statement, it should be said that whoever does a thing is a 'thing-maker'. If they affirm that, they have included thingness under the act and have retracted from their statement that the non-existent is a thing; if they deny it, their question is invalidated.
Source pages p. 924

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of an objection posed by opponents (likely Mu'tazila or Qadariyya) regarding the attribution of creation to the servant. The author begins by stating a principle: the name derived from an action (e.g., 'creator' from 'creating') refers to the one in whom the action's meaning inherently resides, not merely to the one who brings the action into existence. This meaning is the act's being a property (kasb) of the agent, whether the act is voluntary (like free movement) or involuntary (like forced movement). The objection is: if the servant performs a created act, then he is a creator. The author replies: he is a doer (fa'il), but 'creator' (khaliq) requires doing it alone (with exclusive power of invention, ikhtira'). The servant's act is not done alone but has a share of divine creation, so he is an 'acquirer' (muktasib). The author then notes a difference in terminology among those who equate takwin with mukawwan versus those who distinguish. Then he uses a reductio: on the opponents' logic, one who does a thing should be called a 'thing-maker' (mushayyi'), which would lead to absurdities regarding the non-existent (ma'dum). This shows the flaw in their reasoning.

Key Terms
اسم
معنى
كسب
اختراع
خالق
فاعل
مكتسب
تكوين
مكون
مشيء
شيئية
معدوم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If a person does a created act, they are a creator just like God.
Correction: Nasafi explains that 'creator' (khaliq) requires doing the act alone (with exclusive power of invention), whereas the servant's act is not done alone — it is an acquisition (kasb) from God, so the servant is a doer (fa'il) and an acquirer (muktasib), not a creator.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what determines whether someone is called a 'creator' (khaliq)?
A) Performing any act at all
B) Performing an act alone (with exclusive power of invention)
C) Performing an act with divine assistance
D) Being the bringer of the meaning into existence

Key Takeaway
The name of an act belongs to the one in whom the act's meaning truly resides, not to the one who merely brings it into existence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your daily life, when you accomplish something good—like helping someone or achieving a goal—do you ever feel a sense of pride as if you are the sole originator of that act? How does this lesson's distinction between being a 'doer' and a 'creator' challenge your view of your own role? What would it mean for you to truly see your actions as 'acquisitions' from Allah rather than independent creations?
?
The passage argues that the name 'creator' belongs only to the one who does an act alone, with exclusive power. Is there an area in your life where you are claiming ownership or credit for something that you did not bring into existence entirely on your own? How would your humility and gratitude grow if you consistently recognized that every good act of yours is a shared act between you and Allah?
?
When you witness evil or wrongdoing from others, do you find yourself accusing them of being 'creators' of that evil? How does this lesson reframe your understanding of human responsibility? Can you think of a specific situation where you need to adjust your judgment about someone's guilt or blame based on the distinction between doer and creator?

The Najjariyya on Aspects of Actions
Nasafi presents the Najjariyya view of aspects and gives examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Najjariyya argue that a single action can be morally evaluated differently depending on its aspect (jiha), and how they apply this to disbelief, lies, and blackness.

Core Idea
An act can be good in one aspect and bad in another — look at the aspect.

The Najjariyya theological school introduced a sophisticated way of analyzing acts: every act has multiple aspects (jihaat). This lesson presents their view that an act's ugliness or beauty depends on which aspect you consider. You'll read Nasafi's explanation and examples, which show how even disbelief and lies can be considered not ugly from certain aspects, while still being blameworthy from others.

Guided Path
1
Read the Najjariyya claim about aspects
Read the first sentence where they state that an act has aspects, and note the two opposing aspects: as God's act (not ugly) and as servant's act (ugly).
2
Analyze the example of disbelief (kufr)
Examine how disbelief is analyzed: as a thing, accident, belief, and as God's proof. Understand why each aspect does not make it ugly.
If it were ugly as a thing, then everything including faith would be ugly.
3
Consider the example of a lie (kadhib)
Notice that a lie can be evidence of foolishness, and the evidence itself is true (sidq). So from the aspect of truth, it is not ugly; only from the aspect of lying is it ugly.
4
Understand the blackness example and clarification
The blackness example shows that universal categories like 'thing' do not determine specific identity. Also, note that these aspects are not physical directions.
The aspects are conceptual, not spatial.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم النجارية على هذا يزعمون أن الفعل له جهات، فمن حيث إنه فعل الله تعالى ليس بقبيح، ومن حيث إنه فعل العبد قبيح، ويجوز أن يكون للفعل جهات تقبح وتكون معصية ببعض الجهات ولا يكون كذلك ببعض الجهات، ألا ترى أن الكفر شيء وهو عرض وهو اعتقاد، وهو حجة الله تعالى على الكافر بالتعذيب وليس بقبيح، فمن حيث إنه شيء إذ لو كان قبيحًا من حيث إنه شيء لكان كل شيء قبيحًا فكان الإيمان قبيحًا سببًا للعقاب، وكذا كل طاعة، وكذا ليس بقبيح من حيث إنه عرض، ولا من حيث إنه اعتقاد لما يلزم أن يكون الإيمان قبيحًا منهيًا عنه، وكذا الكفر كذب وهو دليل سفه الكافر، ودلالته على سفهه صدق فكان الكذب صدقا في الدلالة على سفه الكاذب وليس بقبيح ولا سفه لاستحقاق العقاب من حيث إنه صدق بل من حيث إنه كذب. وكذا السواد شيء وعرض ولون، وليس بسواد لأنه شيء ولا لأنه عرض ولا لأنه لون لوجودنا أشياء و أعراضا وألوانا ليست بسواد، ولا يعنون بهذه الجهات كما هو جهات الأجسام من حيث فوق وتحت وعن يمين
Then the Najjariyya, upon this, claim that the act has aspects (jihaat): in the aspect that it is the act of God Most High, it is not ugly; and in the aspect that it is the act of the servant, it is ugly. And it is possible that an act has aspects by which it becomes ugly and becomes disobedience in some aspects, and is not so in other aspects. Do you not see that disbelief is a thing, an accident, a belief, and it is the proof of God Most High against the disbeliever for punishment, and it is not ugly? For in the aspect that it is a thing – if it were ugly because it is a thing, then every thing would be ugly, so faith would be ugly and a cause of punishment, and likewise every act of obedience. And likewise, it is not ugly in the aspect that it is an accident, nor in the aspect that it is a belief, because it would entail that faith is ugly and prohibited. And likewise, disbelief is a lie (kadhib) and it is evidence of the disbeliever's foolishness, and its indication of his foolishness is truth (sidq), so the lie becomes truth in indicating the foolishness of the liar – and it is not ugly nor foolishness deserving punishment in the aspect that it is truth, but rather in the aspect that it is a lie. And likewise, blackness is a thing, an accident, and a color, and it is not blackness because it is a thing, nor because it is an accident, nor because it is a color, since we find things, accidents, and colors that are not blackness. And they do not mean by these aspects what is meant by the aspects of bodies like above, below, and right.
Source pages p. 934

Commentary
This passage presents the Najjariyya view (a theological school) on the aspects (jihaat) of actions. According to them, a single act can have multiple aspects: as God's act, it is not ugly; as the servant's act, it is ugly. Moreover, an act can be both disobedience and not disobedience depending on the aspect considered. The author illustrates with disbelief (kufr): it is a thing (shay'), an accident (ʿaraḍ), a belief (iʿtiqād), and it is God's proof against the disbeliever for punishment, yet it is not ugly in all these aspects. For example, being a thing does not make it ugly, otherwise everything would be ugly, including faith. Similarly, being an accident or belief does not entail ugliness. Another example: disbelief is a lie (kadhib) but it is evidence of the disbeliever's foolishness; the indication itself is true (sidq), so from that aspect it is not ugly. The act's ugliness deserving punishment comes from its being a lie, not from its being truth. A final example is blackness (sawād): it is a thing, accident, color, but it is not identified solely by these universal categories; its specific aspect makes it black. The author clarifies that these 'aspects' are not physical directions like up/down/right, but conceptual considerations.

نَجَارِيَّة
جِهَات الْفِعْل
عَرَض
اعْتِقَاد
حُجَّة
كِذْب
صِدْق
سَوَاد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think an act is either wholly good or wholly bad.
Correction: The Najjariyya show it can be good under one aspect and bad under another.

Checkpoint
According to the Najjariyya, in which aspect is disbelief not considered ugly?
A) In the aspect that it is a lie.
B) In the aspect that it is the servant's act.
C) In the aspect that it is God's proof against the disbeliever.
D) In the aspect that it is an accident.

Key Takeaway
The Najjariyya use the concept of aspects to argue that moral qualities depend on the perspective from which an act is considered.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider a sin you have committed, do you ever focus on one 'good' aspect of it to minimize your guilt? How does the Najjariyya teaching on aspects challenge you to honestly acknowledge the full reality of your action before God?
?
The passage shows that even disbelief has aspects that are not ugly, such as being God's proof. Have you ever considered that your own failings, from God's perspective, serve a purpose you cannot fully grasp? How does this thought humble you or change your view of His wisdom?
?
In the example, the truthfulness of the indication (sidq) is distinguished from the lie itself (kadhib). In your daily life, where do you tend to conflate the good consequences of an action with the action itself? Will you now strive to evaluate your deeds by their true moral aspect, not by their outcomes?

The Dilemma of Good Actions: God Must Create Them
The author presents a dilemma forcing Mu'tazila to admit God creates good actions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the author's logical dilemma against the Mu'tazila: if they deny that God creates evil actions, they must affirm that He creates good actions, otherwise they contradict their own principles and the Quran.

Core Idea
If God cannot create evil, He must create good—or face contradiction.

In this passage, the author moves from a conciliatory tone to a direct attack. He exposes a fatal inconsistency in the Mu'tazila position: while they reject that God creates any human actions (to avoid attributing evil to Him), they already believe that acts of obedience are by God's will. This forces them into an inescapable dilemma. Read carefully to see how the author turns their own arguments against them.

Guided Path
1
Understand the context
The author builds on earlier arguments to show that the Mu'tazila cannot avoid affirming that God creates good actions.
This follows a lenient approach; now he becomes direct.
2
Identify the dilemma
The author presents three options: either accept that God creates acts of obedience, or deny that they are by His will, or claim He wills but doesn't create. Each has disastrous consequences for the Mu'tazila.
The dilemma is built on their own admission that good actions are by God's will.
3
See the Quranic argument
The author cites the verse 'Doer of what He wills' to show that whatever is willed by God must be done by Him. The Mu'tazila cannot escape.
This is a direct appeal to scripture.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقف على ما بينا من الوجوه عرف الحقيقة ولم يجبن عن إثبات ذلك عند تهويل المعتزلة، ويبين لهم أنه يقول ما يقولون هم، ويضطر جميع العقلاء إلى القول به. ثم هذا كله منا جرى مع الخصوم على طريق المساهلة فلو مانعناهم وقلنا: هذا منكم تعيين لبعض فصول الخلاف فإن كان المانع لكم من القول بخلق الأفعال بقدر استحالة إضافة القبيح إلى الله تعالى فما المانع لكم في إضافة الطاعات إلى الله تعالى وهي حكم ومحاسن ؟ فقولوا بخلقها لانعدام هذا المانع فيها وإلا ظهر تعنتكم، على أن عندكم كانت الطاعات كلها بإرادة الله تعالى والله تعالى يقول : فعال لما يريد فكانت الطاعات مفعولة له لأنها مرادة له وهو يقول: فعال لما يريد فإما أن يقروا بكون الطاعات مخلوقة له، وإما أن يقولوا إنها ليست بإرادته فيبطلون مذهبهم، وإما أن يقولوا إنها وإن كانت إرادة له فلم يفعلها وينكرون النص ويردونه، وفيه ما فيه. ثم نقول : لا شك أن حسن الطاعات فوق حسن الأجسام، لأن حسنها حسن وهو يختلف باختلاف الحواس على أن عند المعتزلة لا وجه للقول بحسن الأجسام لإنكارهم قبح ما قبح منها وإن عرف ذلك بالحس، فكذا يلزمهم إنكار الحس إذ لا يعرف إلا بما يعرف به القبح، ولا حقيقة لتلك المعرفة فكذا لهذه، وحسن الطاعات عقلي وهو مما لا يختلف باختلاف العقول ولا يستبدل بحال وحال، وتفاضل الفاعلين بتفاضل الأفعال فينبغي أن يكون كل عبد مطيع أفضل من الله تعالى وأحسن فعلاً وأرفع درجة لعلو رتبة فعله على فعل الله تعالى، وانحطاط رتبة فعله عز وجل عن أفعال العباد، ويكون في مقدور العبد من المحاسن ما ليس يماثل ذلك أو يقاربه في مقدور الله تعالى، والقول به قول لا خفاء بما فيه، فكان مقدور الله تعالى هو إبليس والقردة والأقذار والنتان والزمانة والعمى والشلل والأمراض والموت والمصائب ومقدور العبد الصوم والصلاة والحج والإقراض والتصديق واعتقاد التوحيد والإفضال إلى المحتاجين والإحسان إليهم والوفاء بالعهود وأداء الأمانات والجود والسماحة، وغير ذلك من الفضائل الداخلة تحت قدرة العباد، تقدر كل واحد من الفاعلين بمقدور ، وذهب بما خلق وفاز العبد بالقسم الأحسن والحظ الأجزل، وقصرت قدرة القديم عن ذلك، ولعلم المعتزلة بهذا الإلزام يعرضون عن جانب المحاسن صفحًا ويتشبثون بالمقابح ليتمكنوا عسى من ترويج باطلهم على ضعفاء المسلمين عصمنا الله تعالى عن ذلك بكرمه وفضله وهو ذو الفضل العظيم.
He who stops at what we have explained of the aspects knows the reality and does not shy away from affirming that when faced with the intimidation of the Mu'tazila, and he makes clear to them that he says what they say, and all rational people are compelled to affirm it. Then all this from us proceeded with the opponents in a lenient manner. So if we were to oppose them and say: This from you is a specification of some points of disagreement. If your deterrent from saying that actions are created by God is the impossibility of attributing evil to God Almighty, then what is your deterrent regarding attributing acts of obedience to God Almighty, which are wisdom and good things? So say they are created due to the absence of this deterrent in them; otherwise your obstinacy becomes evident. Moreover, according to you, all acts of obedience are by the will of God Almighty, and God Almighty says: 'Doer of what He wills'. So acts of obedience are done by Him because they are willed by Him, and He says: 'Doer of what He wills'. So either they affirm that acts of obedience are created by Him, or they say they are not by His will, thus invalidating their doctrine, or they say that although they are willed by Him, He does not do them, thus denying the text and rejecting it—and therein lies its trouble. Then we say: There is no doubt that the goodness of acts of obedience is superior to the goodness of bodies, because their goodness is intellectual and it does not vary with the variation of senses. However, according to the Mu'tazila, there is no basis for saying that bodies are good, because they deny the ugliness of what is ugly among them, even if that is known by sense. So likewise they are compelled to deny sense, since it is only known by what ugliness is known by, and there is no reality to that knowledge, so likewise to this. The goodness of acts of obedience is intellectual, and it is something that does not vary with the variation of intellects nor changes from state to state. The superiority of agents is by the superiority of actions. So it must be that every obedient servant is better than God Almighty, and better in action, and higher in rank, because the rank of his action is above the action of God Almighty, and the rank of His action—glorified and exalted—is lower than the actions of servants. And the servant has in his power of good things what is not similar to or near that in the power of God Almighty. Saying that is a saying whose content is not hidden. So the power of God Almighty is Iblis, apes, filth, stench, decrepitude, blindness, paralysis, diseases, death, and calamities. The power of the servant is fasting, prayer, pilgrimage, lending, believing, affirmation of divine unity, bestowing upon the needy, kindness to them, fulfilling covenants, rendering trusts, generosity, magnanimity, and other virtues that fall under the power of servants. Each of the agents is apportioned according to what is in his power, and he departs with what he created. The servant obtains the best portion and the greatest lot, and the power of the Eternal falls short of that. Because the Mu'tazila know of this necessary consequence, they turn away from the side of good things completely and cling to ugly things, hoping perhaps to market their falsehood among weak Muslims. May God Almighty protect us from that through His generosity and bounty, and He is the possessor of great bounty.
Source pages p. 937

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the Mu'tazila concerning the creation of human actions. The author first notes that anyone who understands the previous arguments will not be intimidated by the Mu'tazila's blustering. He then presents a softening concession (on the path of leniency) before launching a more direct attack. The argument is: if the Mu'tazila deny that God creates all actions because creating evil is impossible for God, then they must affirm that God creates acts of obedience (since those are good, and no such impossibility exists). If they refuse, they are being obstinate. Moreover, they already believe that all acts of obedience are by God's will (Quran 11:107), so they must admit either that God creates them (contradicting their denial), or that they are not by His will (invalidating their own doctrine), or that God wills them but does not create them (rejecting the plain sense of the Quran). The author then goes further: the goodness of acts of obedience is intellectual and superior to the sensory goodness of bodies, so it is even more necessary that God create them. The Mu'tazila's denial leads to absurd conclusions: if actions are not created by God, then human actions are better than God's actions, making the obedient servant superior to God in action and rank. God's power would then be limited to ugly, base things (like Iblis, disease, etc.) while human power encompasses virtues like prayer, charity, and belief. This is manifestly false. The Mu'tazila, aware of this forced consequence, avoid discussing good actions and instead cling to the problem of evil, hoping to deceive the weak-minded. The passage concludes with a prayer for protection.

قبيح

طاعات
إرادة
فعال لما يريد

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Mu'tazila deny that God creates any human action because evil actions are unworthy of God.
Correction: The author argues that this logic also forces the Mu'tazila to affirm that God creates good actions, since there is no similar objection regarding good. They cannot have it both ways.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the Mu'tazila's stated reason for denying that God creates actions?
A) They believe actions are eternal.
B) They cannot attribute evil to God.
C) They follow the Quran literally.
D) They think human free will is absolute.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila's argument against God creating actions fails because it forces them into a logical contradiction regarding good actions.

?

The Absurdity: Servant Superior to God?
The author uses reductio ad absurdum to show the Mu'tazila position implies human superiority over God.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author demonstrates that denying God's creation of good actions leads to the absurd conclusion that human actions are superior to God's, and that God's power is limited to base things while humans possess noble virtues.

Core Idea
If humans create good actions, they surpass God—an impossible contradiction.

The author now takes the Mu'tazila's position to its logical extreme. If God does not create good actions, then those actions must be created by humans. But since actions reflect the agent's excellence, human actions would then be superior to God's actions, making the servant better than God. Moreover, God's power would be confined to creating evil and base things, while humans create virtue. This is manifestly absurd. See how the author forces the Mu'tazila into this repugnant conclusion.

Guided Path
1
Compare goodness of actions vs bodies
The author argues that intellectual goodness (of acts of obedience) is superior to sensory goodness (of bodies). Since Mu'tazila deny God creates actions, they must also deny sensory goodness consistently, but that leads to denying sense itself.
This is a subtle argument about epistemology.
2
Trace the implication for agents' rank
If actions are not created by God, then human actions are independent and indicate the agent's perfection. This leads to the conclusion that any obedient servant is better than God in action and rank.
This is a direct consequence of their position.
3
Observe the contrast in what each power contains
God's power would only encompass evil things (Iblis, disease, etc.) while human power encompasses all virtues (prayer, charity, etc.). This is blasphemous.
The author uses vivid examples to highlight the absurdity.
4
Understand the Mu'tazila's evasion
The author notes that the Mu'tazila are aware of this forced consequence and therefore avoid discussing good actions altogether, focusing only on evil to deceive weak Muslims.
This reveals their dishonesty.
